The Absolute Impossibility of Transforming Necessary Knowledge
The second school's proof for absolute impossibility of transformation of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second school's arguments for the absolute impossibility of necessary knowledge transforming into theoretical knowledge, including the direct proof from the impossibility of being devoid of necessary truths, the indirect proof from the existence of theoretical sciences, and the methodological critique of relying on intuition.

Core Idea
Transformation of necessary knowledge is absolutely impossible because it would unravel all rational inquiry.

This passage presents a deeper layer of the debate on the transformation of knowledge. Having established that some theologians maintain absolute impossibility, Sanusi now provides the proof structure: a direct argument from the inability to be devoid of necessary truths, an indirect argument from the existence of theoretical sciences, and a subtle critique of relying on intuition. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get an overall sense of the argument's flow before diving into details.
2
Identify the two main proofs
The passage offers two arguments: first, a direct argument that transformation would require the inquirer to be devoid of all necessary knowledge, which is impossible by intuition. Second, an indirect argument: if transformation were possible in one, it would be possible in all, leading to the impossibility of any theoretical science, contradicting the existence of such sciences.
3
Note the objection and its handling
The direct argument faces an objection: transformation might only occur in non-essential necessary truths. The second argument implicitly addresses this by showing that if any necessary truth can transform, then all can, leading to a universal breakdown.
The second argument is more robust against this objection.
4
Reflect on the concluding remark
Sanusi notes that the proof would be stronger if it relied on logical impossibility rather than intuition, because intuition only shows that we are not actually devoid, not that it is impossible to be so.
This highlights the difference between de facto and de jure impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الثاني : وعليه آخرون من المتكلمين لا يجوز مطلقاً وإلا لجاز الخلو عن الضروري إذ قد مر أن النظر ينافي العلم بالمطلوب المنظور فيه فإذا انقلب الضروري نظرياً وجب أن يكون الناظر في ذلك النظري خالياً عن العلم به، وذلك يؤدي إلى جواز خلو العاقل الناظر في العلوم عن العلم باستحالة اجتماع الضدين، وبأنه لا واسطة بين النفي والإثبات وبأن الكل أعظم من الجزء إلى غير ذلك من الضروريات التي تلزم العاقل، وأنه محال بالوجدان الشاهد بأن أمثال ما ذكر من البديهيات يستحيل انفكاك العاقل عنها، وفيه بحث لجواز أن يكون الانقلاب فيما عداها من الضروريات التي يجوز فقدانها، وقد يستدل لهذا المذهب بأنه لو جاز الانقلاب في الضروري لجاز في الكل، وما هو جائز لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فلنفرض أن الضروريات انقلبت نظرية وحينئذ يستحيل حصول شيء من العلوم النظرية، إذ لا بد من انتهائها إلى العلم الضروري دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وفيه ما قد عرفته آنفاً وأيضاً حصول العلوم النظرية واقع فدل على أن ذلك التقدير أعني : انقلاب الضروريات جميع جميع العلوم الضرورية التي هي شرط لأهلية النظر كما مر فيلزم أن يكون تلك العلوم شرطاً، وأن لا يكون شرطاً، وأنه محال وبهذا تبين أنه لو اكتفى بقوله : وأنه محال ولم يقيده بقوله : بالوجدان لكان أسد لئلا يرد البحث بأن الوجدان إنما يدل على عدم الخلو لا على عدم جوازه.
The second school: and upon it are others from the theologians: it is not permitted absolutely, otherwise it would be possible to be devoid of the necessary, for it has passed that inquiry contradicts knowledge of the sought object of inquiry. So if the necessary becomes theoretical, the inquirer in that theoretical matter must be devoid of knowledge of it, and that leads to the possibility of the rational inquirer in the sciences being devoid of knowledge of the impossibility of the conjunction of two opposites, and that there is no intermediary between negation and affirmation, and that the whole is greater than the part, and other necessary matters that are incumbent upon the rational [inquirer]. And it is impossible by intuition, which attests that examples like those mentioned of self-evident truths cannot be absent from the rational person. And in it there is discussion because it is possible that the transformation be in other than these necessary matters which it is permissible to lack. And it may be argued for this school that if transformation were possible in the necessary, it would be possible in all. And what is possible, from the assumption of its occurrence no impossibility necessarily follows. So let us assume that the necessary matters became theoretical, and then it would be impossible to obtain any theoretical science, since they must terminate in necessary knowledge to avoid regress and infinite series. And in it there is what you already know. Also, the occurrence of theoretical sciences is real, so it indicates that that estimation, namely the transformation of all necessary sciences which are a condition for the capability of inquiry as mentioned, entails that those sciences would be a condition and not a condition, and that is impossible. And by this it becomes clear that if he had been content with his statement 'and it is impossible' and did not qualify it with 'by intuition', it would be more sound, lest the objection arise that intuition only indicates non-absence, not the impossibility of [absence].
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the second school's view that transformation of necessary knowledge into theoretical knowledge is absolutely impossible. The proof is: if it were possible, it would lead to the possibility of a rational thinker being entirely devoid of all necessary knowledge (e.g., the impossibility of opposites, the whole being greater than the part, etc.), which is impossible by intuitive experience (wijdān). An objection is raised: transformation might be possible only for non-essential necessary truths. The passage then provides another indirect proof: if transformation were possible in one necessary truth, it would be possible in all; assuming all necessary truths become theoretical, no theoretical science could be established due to infinite regress, but theoretical sciences do exist, so the assumption is false. The passage concludes that the argument would be stronger if it relied solely on impossibility without appealing to intuition, since intuition only shows de facto absence, not impossibility.

Key Terms
المذهب الثاني
لا يجوز
الخلو عن الضروري
النظري
الضروري
الوجدان
انقلاب
أهلية النظر
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the impossibility of transformation is proven solely by intuition (wijdān).
Correction: The passage shows that intuition only indicates de facto absence, not absurdity. The stronger proof is the logical contradiction that arises from assuming transformation of all necessary truths.

Checkpoint
What is the main proof given for the second school's position that transformation of necessary knowledge is impossible?
A) Transformation would require an infinite regress of theoretical sciences.
B) It would lead to the possibility of a rational inquirer being devoid of all necessary knowledge, which is impossible.
C) Intuition shows that no necessary knowledge ever becomes theoretical.
D) The occurrence of theoretical sciences proves that necessary knowledge must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The absolute impossibility of transforming necessary knowledge is proven by both a direct argument (impossible to be devoid of necessary truths) and an indirect argument (transformation would undermine all theoretical science), with the latter being more logically robust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that without necessary knowledge, all inquiry collapses. Reflect on one belief you hold that you consider self-evidently true—like the impossibility of contradiction. Have you ever actually examined that belief, or have you merely assumed it? If you were forced to prove it, could you? What would it mean for your broader worldview if that foundation were shaken? Are you willing to trace your every conviction back to its roots, or do you prefer the comfort of unexamined certainty?
?
The author notes that intuition only proves we are not devoid of necessary knowledge, not that it is impossible to be devoid. This means your certainty about many things might be based on habit, not proof. Name one Islamic belief you hold that you have never seriously doubted. Is your confidence in it based on a logical proof you could articulate, or on the fact that you've never been in a situation where you needed to defend it? If a sincere, well-argued challenge to that belief appeared, would you be able to respond with more than 'I just know it's true'? This lesson demands you prepare an answer.
?
The indirect argument shows that if necessary truths could transform, all knowledge would be impossible. Yet you live your life relying on knowledge every day. How many of your daily assumptions—that the sun will rise, that your actions have consequences, that truth matters—are actually necessary truths? If you discovered one of them was merely theoretical, would you feel a sense of betrayal? This lesson asks: on what foundation are you actually building your life, and is it strong enough to hold the weight of your eternal soul?

The Intuition Argument against Transforming Necessary Knowledge
The author presents the argument from intuition against transformation of necessary knowledge and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from immediate intuition against the transformation of necessary knowledge and the logical objection that distinguishes among different types of necessary truths.

Core Idea
Intuition shows some truths are necessary, but not all necessary truths are equally binding.

This lesson examines a key argument in Islamic epistemology: that some knowledge is so self-evident that it cannot be transformed into mere theoretical knowledge. The argument appeals to intuition (الوجدان) and self-evident truths like 'the whole is greater than the part.' However, the author raises a logical objection concerning the scope of the claim. Let's read the passage and unpack its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice the shift from presenting the argument to the objection indicated by 'وفيه بحث'.
2
Identify the main claim and its evidence
The passage claims it is impossible (محال) for a rational person to be devoid of self-evident truths. Find the phrase that states this and the evidence from intuition.
The evidence is 'بالوجدان الشاهد' — by intuition that testifies.
3
Understand the objection (بحث)
The objection says the transformation might occur in necessary truths that are not of this self-evident kind. The claimant's universal negative claim can be refuted by a particular affirmative.
The logical form: universal negative (all necessary truths are impossible to transform) can be disproven by showing one counterexample.
4
Reflect on the difference among necessary truths
The passage denies that there is no difference among necessary truths; some are not always necessary (غير لازم). Consider what this means for the argument.
Not all necessary truths are equally binding or universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واسطة بين النفي والإثبات وبأن الكل أعظم من الجزء إلى غير ذلك من الضروريات التي تلزم العاقل، وأنه محال بالوجدان الشاهد بأن أمثال ما ذكر من البديهيات يستحيل انفكاك العاقل عنها، وفيه بحث لجواز أن يكون الانقلاب فيما عداها من الضروريات التي يجوز فقدانها. قوله: ( وفيه بحث إلخ لأن مدعى المستدل السالبة الكلية فيجوز رفعها بتحقق الموجبة الجزئية، وعدم الفرق الذي ادعى المستدل بين الضروريات ممنوع؛ كيف وبعضها غير لازم.
Intermediate between negation and affirmation, and that the whole is greater than the part, and other necessary truths that are binding upon the rational person, and that it is impossible by intuition which testifies that examples of the self-evident truths mentioned cannot be separated from the rational person. But there is a discussion because the transformation could be in other necessary truths that may be absent. His saying: (But there is a discussion etc.) because the claimant's claim is a universal negative, so it can be lifted by a particular affirmative, and the claimant's claim that there is no difference among necessary truths is denied; how? Because some of them are not necessary.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from intuition (الوجدان) for the impossibility of transforming necessary knowledge into theoretical knowledge. The argument states that it is impossible, as known by immediate intuition, for a rational person to be devoid of self-evident truths like 'the whole is greater than the part'. The author then notes a discussion (بحث): the transformation might be possible in necessary truths that are not of this self-evident kind, which can be absent. The commentary explains that the claimant (of impossibility) asserts a universal negative ('all necessary knowledge is impossible to transform'), which can be refuted by a particular affirmative (showing at least one case where it can transform). The defense denies that there is no difference among necessary truths, pointing out that some are not necessary (i.e., not always present).

Key Terms
الضروريات
الوجدان
البديهيات
السالبة الكلية
الموجبة الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All necessary knowledge is equally impossible to transform.
Correction: The passage shows that only those necessary truths that are absolutely self-evident and binding are claimed impossible by intuition; other necessary truths may be absent and thus could potentially transform.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the argument that transformation of necessary knowledge is impossible?
A) Logical deduction from regress
B) Immediate intuition (الوجدان)
C) Consensus of the mutakallimun
D) The authority of the Quran

Key Takeaway
The intuition argument claims impossibility based on self-evident truths, but the objection shows that not all necessary truths are equally binding, so the universal claim is refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely hold many beliefs you consider absolutely certain. Which one of these, if questioned, would you be most afraid to lose? What would that fear reveal about whether that belief is truly self-evident or just comfortable?
?
The argument shows that a universal negative can be shattered by a single counterexample. Is there a person or group you've dismissed as incapable of understanding a truth you hold? Could your dismissal be based on a false universal claim? What would it take for you to honestly re-examine that judgment?
?
Some necessary truths are binding on all rational minds, but others are not. Which of your core beliefs might actually be in the second category—truths you treat as absolute but that others rationally reject? How would your life change if you admitted that possibility? Are you willing to explore that?

The Regress Proof Against Transformation of Necessary Knowledge
A proof from hypothetical assumption and regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the regress proof that argues necessary knowledge cannot transform into theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
If necessary knowledge could transform, all knowledge would become impossible.

This lesson presents a philosophical argument that attempts to prove that necessary knowledge cannot change into theoretical knowledge. The argument uses a conditional and assumes that if transformation is possible for one necessary truth, it must be possible for all, leading to a regress that would make knowledge impossible. We will examine the steps and the underlying assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. The passage presents a conditional argument.
Notice the phrase 'لو جاز الانقلاب في الضروري لجاز في الكل'.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The proof begins with a conditional: if it is permissible for one necessary truth to transform, then it is permissible for all. This is a key logical leap.
This premise is not universally accepted, as the critique will show.
3
Understand the hypothetical scenario
The proof then assumes the scenario where all necessary truths have transformed into theoretical. What would be the consequence?
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that no theoretical knowledge could be obtained because all theoretical knowledge ultimately depends on necessary knowledge to avoid circularity and infinite regress.
This conclusion is based on the assumption that the transformation is global.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل لهذا المذهب بأنه لو جاز الانقلاب في الضروري لجاز في الكل، وما هو جائز لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فلنفرض أن الضروريات انقلبت نظرية وحينئذ يستحيل حصول شيء من العلوم النظرية، إذ لا بد من انتهائها إلى العلم الضروري دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وفيه ما قد عرفته آنفاً. قوله: ( وحينئذ يستحيل إلخ) فيه بحث لأنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان انقلاب جميع الضرورية متعاقبة، إذ يجوز مع ذلك الانقلاب انقلاب النظري ضرورياً أيضاً، كما لا يخفى. قوله: ( ما قد عرفته من منع الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : لو جاز الانقلاب في ضروري لجاز في الكل. نظرية غير واقع وأما أنه مستحيل، فلا دلالة عليه أصلاً.
And a proof is given for this doctrine: if transformation were permissible in a necessary [truth], it would be permissible in all, and what is permissible does not necessitate that assuming its occurrence is impossible. So let us assume that necessary truths have transformed into theoretical, then it becomes impossible to obtain any theoretical knowledge, because theoretical knowledge must terminate in necessary knowledge to avoid circularity and infinite regress. But there is in it what you already know. His saying: (Then it becomes impossible etc.) there is a discussion because that would only follow if the transformation of all necessary [truths] occurred consecutively; for it is possible with that transformation that theoretical [knowledge] also transforms into necessary, as is not hidden. His saying: (What you already know is the denial of the entailment derived from his saying: if transformation were permissible in a necessary it would be permissible in all. Theoretical [knowledge] does not actually occur? But as for it being impossible, there is no indication of it at all.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the impossibility of transforming necessary knowledge into theoretical. The proof argues by conditional: if it were permissible for one necessary truth to transform, it would be permissible for all, and since it is permissible to assume it happens, we can suppose all necessary truths become theoretical. Then no theoretical knowledge could be obtained because all theoretical knowledge must ultimately depend on necessary knowledge to avoid circularity and infinite regress. The commentary objects that this only follows if the transformation of all necessary truths happens simultaneously; it is possible that as some necessary truths transform into theoretical, some theoretical knowledge transforms into necessary, thus providing a foundation. Another commentary denies the entailment that if one transforms then all must, as some necessary truths may be exempt. The passage ends by stating that the assumption that all theoretical knowledge is not actual and that [transformation] is impossible has no proof.

Key Terms
الانقلاب
العلوم النظرية
العلوم الضرورية
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would happen if all necessary truths transformed into theoretical?
A) Theoretical knowledge would still be possible.
B) No theoretical knowledge could be obtained.
C) Necessary knowledge would remain.
D) Circularity would be avoided.

Key Takeaway
The regress proof argues that if necessary knowledge could transform into theoretical, then all knowledge would become impossible, because theoretical knowledge ultimately depends on necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumptions do you hold about matters of faith that you have never questioned? Are you willing to examine whether they are truly necessary knowledge or merely inherited opinions?
?
If you were to lose all certainty in your foundational beliefs today, would your entire worldview collapse? What does that tell you about the reliance of your knowledge on unchanging truths?
?
When you present a proof for something you believe, do you ensure it ultimately rests on something you hold as necessary and self-evident, or do you rely on endless chains of reasoning that could be doubted?

Critique of the Regress Proof
Critique of the regress proof via objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main critiques of the regress proof: that it only works if the transformation is simultaneous, and that the premise of universal permissibility is denied.

Core Idea
The regress proof is not decisive; it depends on assumptions about the manner of transformation.

The previous lesson presented a proof that necessary knowledge cannot become theoretical. This lesson examines two objections: first, that the proof only holds if all necessary truths transform consecutively, which is not necessarily the case; second, that the initial premise that transformation in one implies transformation in all is denied. We will see how these critiques weaken the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation, which contains two objections and a final remark.
Look for the markers 'قوله: ( وحينئذ يستحيل إلخ)' and 'قوله: ( ما قد عرفته من منع الملازمة)'.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection points out that the proof's impossibility only follows if the transformation of all necessary truths is consecutive. It might be possible that as some necessary truths become theoretical, some theoretical knowledge becomes necessary.
The Arabic says 'إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان انقلاب جميع الضرورية متعاقبة'.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection denies the entailment from 'permissible in one' to 'permissible in all'. The critic argues that this is not necessarily true; some necessary truths might be exempt.
This objection attacks the very premise of the proof.
4
Understand the final statement
The passage ends with a remark that the assumption that theoretical knowledge is 'not actual' (or that transformation is impossible) has no proof.
This is a strong statement against the proof's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل لهذا المذهب بأنه لو جاز الانقلاب في الضروري لجاز في الكل، وما هو جائز لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فلنفرض أن الضروريات انقلبت نظرية وحينئذ يستحيل حصول شيء من العلوم النظرية، إذ لا بد من انتهائها إلى العلم الضروري دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وفيه ما قد عرفته آنفاً. قوله: ( وحينئذ يستحيل إلخ) فيه بحث لأنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان انقلاب جميع الضرورية متعاقبة، إذ يجوز مع ذلك الانقلاب انقلاب النظري ضرورياً أيضاً، كما لا يخفى. قوله: ( ما قد عرفته من منع الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : لو جاز الانقلاب في ضروري لجاز في الكل. نظرية غير واقع وأما أنه مستحيل، فلا دلالة عليه أصلاً.
And a proof is given for this doctrine: if transformation were permissible in a necessary [truth], it would be permissible in all, and what is permissible does not necessitate that assuming its occurrence is impossible. So let us assume that necessary truths have transformed into theoretical, then it becomes impossible to obtain any theoretical knowledge, because theoretical knowledge must terminate in necessary knowledge to avoid circularity and infinite regress. But there is in it what you already know. His saying: (Then it becomes impossible etc.) there is a discussion because that would only follow if the transformation of all necessary [truths] occurred consecutively; for it is possible with that transformation that theoretical [knowledge] also transforms into necessary, as is not hidden. His saying: (What you already know is the denial of the entailment derived from his saying: if transformation were permissible in a necessary it would be permissible in all. Theoretical [knowledge] does not actually occur? But as for it being impossible, there is no indication of it at all.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the impossibility of transforming necessary knowledge into theoretical. The proof argues by conditional: if it were permissible for one necessary truth to transform, it would be permissible for all, and since it is permissible to assume it happens, we can suppose all necessary truths become theoretical. Then no theoretical knowledge could be obtained because all theoretical knowledge must ultimately depend on necessary knowledge to avoid circularity and infinite regress. The commentary objects that this only follows if the transformation of all necessary truths happens simultaneously; it is possible that as some necessary truths transform into theoretical, some theoretical knowledge transforms into necessary, thus providing a foundation. Another commentary denies the entailment that if one transforms then all must, as some necessary truths may be exempt. The passage ends by stating that the assumption that all theoretical knowledge is not actual and that [transformation] is impossible has no proof.

Key Terms
الملازمة
الانقلاب
النظري
الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The regress proof is conclusive and cannot be refuted.
Correction: The critique shows that the proof depends on assumptions about the manner of transformation and the universal applicability of the conditional.

Checkpoint
What is one critique of the regress proof mentioned in the passage?
A) That the transformation is impossible.
B) That it only works if the transformation is simultaneous.
C) That necessary knowledge does not exist.
D) That theoretical knowledge is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The regress proof is not without flaw; its conclusions depend on specific assumptions about the nature of transformation and the logical entailment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief you consider necessary and unquestionable, are you genuinely open to the possibility that it might rest on assumptions you have not examined? How does this passage's critique of the regress proof challenge your own certainty about any of your core beliefs? What assumption in your own faith or worldview might you need to reexamine today?
?
The passage suggests that theoretical knowledge can transform into necessary truth. Reflect on a belief that started as mere theory for you—something you learned or reasoned to—but later became a deep certainty that shaped your actions. What caused that transformation? Are you actively seeking such transformations in your understanding of Allah and His attributes?
?
This lesson reveals that even scholars can overlook hidden entailments and assumptions in arguments. Have you ever accepted a religious argument simply because it sounded logical, without scrutinizing its underlying premises? What will you do differently in your pursuit of knowledge to avoid such oversight, especially on matters of aqeedah?

The Third Doctrine: Foundational Necessary Knowledge Cannot Transform
Al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn argue that necessary knowledge conditioning intellectual perfection cannot transform.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection cannot transform into theoretical knowledge, according to the third doctrinal position, and how this differs from other necessary truths.

Core Idea
Some necessary truths are so foundational that transforming them would make them conditions for themselves.

This passage introduces a nuanced position on the limits of rational transformation. While earlier doctrines allowed or restricted transformation differently, the third doctrine identifies a specific category of necessary knowledge—that which conditions the very functioning of the intellect—as immune to transformation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the hierarchical relationship between necessary and theoretical knowledge in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two clear parts: the rule for necessary knowledge that is a condition, and the exception for what is not.
2
Identify the two types of necessary knowledge
The doctrine distinguishes between necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection and necessary knowledge that is not. Underline or note the phrases that indicate this distinction.
3
Follow the logical chain for the conditioning type
Trace the reasoning: Intellectual perfection conditions theoretical reasoning, which conditions theoretical knowledge. If necessary knowledge transforms into theoretical knowledge, it would become a condition for itself—an impossibility.
The passage says it would be 'prior to itself by three ranks'.
4
Note the exception and the author's remark
For necessary knowledge that does not condition intellectual perfection, transformation is permissible. The author ends with 'وقد عرفت ما فيه,' hinting at a problem with this position. Consider what that critique might be.
This remark shows that the author is not entirely satisfied with the doctrine.
5
Compare with the first doctrine
Earlier the first doctrine held that transformation is permissible in all cases. Contrast the two positions. Why might the third doctrine be more restrictive?
This helps you see the spectrum of views on transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الثالث: وهو قول آخر للقاضي وعليه إمام الحرمين لا يجوز الانقلاب في ضروري هو شرط لكمال العقل، إذ العقل أي كماله شرط للنظر فإنه لا يتم إلا به وهو أي النظر شرط للنظري لتوقفه عليه فيكون النظري أعني الضروري المذكور الذي انقلب نظرياً شرطاً لنفسه ومتقدماً عليه بمراتب ثلاث بخلاف الضروري الذي ليس شرطاً لكمال العقل فإنه يجوز انقلابه نظرياً لما مر في المذهب الأول، وقد عرفت ما فيه
The third doctrine: it is another saying of al-Qadi and followed by Imam al-Haramayn that transformation is not permissible in a necessary [truth] that is a condition for the perfection of the intellect. For the intellect, i.e., its perfection, is a condition for theoretical reasoning, as theoretical reasoning cannot be completed without it, and it, i.e., theoretical reasoning, is a condition for theoretical knowledge because it depends on it. So the theoretical [knowledge], meaning the aforementioned necessary [truth] that transformed into theoretical, becomes a condition for itself and prior to itself by three ranks. Contrary to the necessary [truth] that is not a condition for the perfection of the intellect, its transformation into theoretical is permissible as mentioned in the first doctrine, and you have known what is in it.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the third doctrine (المذهب الثالث) attributed to al-Qadi and endorsed by Imam al-Haramayn. It distinguishes between two types of necessary knowledge: (1) that which is a condition for the perfection of the intellect (كمال العقل), and (2) that which is not. The argument is that if the first type transformed into theoretical knowledge, it would become a condition for itself, because the perfection of the intellect conditions theoretical reasoning (النظر), which conditions theoretical knowledge (النظري). So the transformed necessary knowledge (now theoretical) would be both the condition and the conditioned, leading to a vicious circle. Therefore such transformation is impossible. For the second type, transformation is permissible, as established in the first doctrine. The author adds a critical remark 'وقد عرفت ما فيه' indicating that there is a problem with this position.

Key Terms
العقل
كمال العقل
النظر
النظري
الشرط
كمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All necessary knowledge is equally impossible to transform.
Correction: The third doctrine specifically targets necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection; other necessary knowledge may transform.

Checkpoint
According to the third doctrine, why is it impossible for necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection to transform into theoretical?
A) Because it would lead to a contradiction of being a condition for itself
B) Because it is impossible by intuition
C) Because the Mu'tazila deny it
D) Because it would make theoretical reasoning unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The third doctrine restricts transformation only for necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection, because such transformation would create a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This passage warns that transforming such a belief into a theoretical one would create a circular reliance on reason. Are you willing to examine even your most sacred assumptions, or do you fear what you might find?
?
The author hints that the third doctrine has a flaw ('وقد عرفت ما فيه'). Are you comfortable with the idea that even the most rigorous human reasoning about divine matters may contain hidden weaknesses? How does that awareness affect your trust in your own intellect versus your reliance on revelation?
?
If some necessary truths about God cannot be transformed without circularity, what does that say about your need for direct divine guidance rather than purely rational deduction? Are there areas in your faith where you have substituted reasoning for submission? Take a moment to repent for any intellectual pride.

The Third Madhab on Conversion of Necessary to Theoretical
Al-Jurjani outlines the third madhab on conversion of necessary to theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third madhab's ruling on the conversion of necessary knowledge to theoretical and its reasoning based on circularity.

Core Idea
When necessary knowledge is a condition for the intellect, its conversion to theoretical creates a self-conditioning impossibility.

Al-Jurjani now presents the third school of thought regarding whether necessary knowledge can become theoretical. This position, held by al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, makes a crucial distinction: not all necessary knowledge is subject to conversion. Only that which is a condition for the perfection of the intellect is protected from conversion, lest it become a condition for itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third madhab
Carefully read the Arabic passage starting from 'نظرية غير واقع' up to 'وقد عرفت ما فيه'. This is the core of the lesson.
The opening phrase 'نظرية غير واقع' may be unclear due to OCR; focus on the argument that follows.
2
Identify the key condition
Notice that the third madhab distinguishes between necessary knowledge that is a condition for the perfection of the intellect and that which is not. Find this distinction in the text.
3
Trace the circularity argument
The argument states that if such necessary knowledge became theoretical, it would be a condition for itself. Follow the chain: intellect's perfection → condition for theoretical inquiry → condition for theoretical knowledge.
The text mentions 'ثلاث بمراتب' (three ranks) of priority.
4
Compare with other madhabs
Understand why conversion is allowed for other necessary knowledge, referencing the first madhab as mentioned in the passage.

Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the third madhab (school) on the conversion of necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī) into theoretical knowledge (naẓarī). The author first notes that the claim of impossibility has no proof. The third madhab, attributed to al-Qadi and endorsed by Imam al-Haramayn, states that conversion is not permissible for necessary knowledge that is a condition for the perfection of the intellect. The reasoning is that the intellect's perfection is a condition for theoretical inquiry (naẓar), and theoretical inquiry is a condition for theoretical knowledge. Therefore, if that necessary knowledge became theoretical, it would become a condition for itself, creating a circular priority. In contrast, necessary knowledge that is not a condition for intellectual perfection may be converted into theoretical, as per the first madhab. The passage then discusses the conversion of theoretical into necessary knowledge, which is agreed upon as permissible by all theologians. According to the Ash'aris, this occurs when God creates necessary knowledge related to that theoretical knowledge. The Mu'tazila, however, while agreeing on permissibility, deny its actual occurrence in knowledge of God and His attributes, because such knowledge is obligatory for the servant; if it were necessary, it would not be within the servant's power, making obligation evil according to their principles.

العلم النظري

C) Because the Mu'tazila object to it.
D) Because the first madhab already established permissibility.

Key Takeaway
The third madhab forbids converting necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection into theoretical knowledge, as it would lead to circularity.

?

?
This lesson warns about circular reasoning when a condition for the intellect is converted into theoretical knowledge. Will you now be more careful to distinguish between necessary and theoretical knowledge in your own thinking?

The author argues that some necessary knowledge cannot be converted because it would create a self-conditioning paradox. Is there a truth in your life that you rely on to think clearly—but that you have never examined? What would it mean for you to finally question it? Commit to examining one such belief this week.

Conversion of Theoretical to Necessary: Agreement and Mu'tazila Objection
Al-Jurjani discusses the conversion of theoretical to necessary and the Mu'tazila objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the agreement on permissibility of converting theoretical knowledge to necessary, and the Mu'tazila's objection regarding knowledge of God and His attributes.

Core Idea
Theologians agree theoretical can become necessary by God's act, but Mu'tazila deny it for knowledge of God due to obligation.

After discussing the conversion of necessary to theoretical, al-Jurjani turns to the reverse: theoretical knowledge becoming necessary. All theologians agree this is possible in principle, but a critical difference emerges when it comes to knowledge of God and His attributes. The Mu'tazila argue that if such knowledge were necessary, it would not be within human power, making the obligation impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on conversion of theoretical to necessary
Read from 'وأما انقلاب النظري ضرورياً فجائز' to the end of the passage. This covers the agreement and the Mu'tazila objection.
The Arabic may be cut off at the end; focus on the argument presented.
2
Identify the point of agreement
Note that all theologians (mutakallimun) agree on the permissibility of conversion of theoretical to necessary. Find the phrase 'اتفاقاً من المتكلمين'.
3
Understand the Ash'ari mechanism
According to the Ash'aris, this conversion occurs by God creating necessary knowledge about the theoretical matter. The text says 'بأن يخلق الله تعالى علماً ضرورياً متعلقاً به'.
4
Examine the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila agree on permissibility but deny actual occurrence for knowledge of God and His attributes. Their reasoning: such knowledge is obligatory for the servant, and if it were necessary, it would not be within the servant's power, making the obligation morally evil (qabīḥ).

The Dispute over Necessary Knowledge's Basis
Al-Jurjani presents the Ash'ari dispute on necessary knowledge's basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions within the Ash'ari school regarding whether necessary knowledge can be based on theoretical knowledge, and the key arguments for each side.

Core Idea
Can necessary knowledge depend on theory? The Ash'aris split on this foundational question.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif dives into a nuanced dispute within Ash'arism: can knowledge we consider necessary (like the impossibility of opposites) actually be grounded in theoretical reasoning? Al-Jurjani presents both camps and their rationales, challenging us to think about the foundations of certainty. Understanding this debate illuminates how classical theologians grappled with the relationship between self-evident truths and reasoned inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage carefully and note that the author first states there is no dispute that theoretical knowledge is based on necessary knowledge, but the reverse is disputed. Then, two groups are mentioned: some Ash'aris who deny, and some who permit.
The denial camp is mentioned first, the permission camp second.
2
Trace the argument of the deniers
Find the reasoning for denial: if necessary knowledge were based on theoretical, it would depend on that theoretical knowledge and thus not be necessary—a contradiction.
The term 'خلف' indicates a logical contradiction.
3
Trace the argument of the permiters
Find the reasoning for permission: knowledge of the impossibility of opposites is built on the existence of opposites, but knowledge of their existence is not necessary because opposition only occurs among accidents, and knowledge of accidents is established by proof, not necessary.
This shows that even necessary-seeming knowledge may rest on non-necessary foundations.

Commentary
This passage addresses the question: can necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī) be based on theoretical knowledge (al-‘ilm al-naẓarī)? It notes that there is no disagreement that theoretical knowledge is based on necessary knowledge, but the reverse is disputed. Some Ash'aris deny it, arguing that if necessary knowledge were based on theoretical, it would depend on that theoretical knowledge and thus not be necessary, which is a contradiction. Others permit it, using the example of knowledge of the impossibility of opposites: this knowledge is built on the existence of the two opposites, but knowledge of their existence is not necessary because opposition only occurs among accidents, and knowledge of accidents is not necessary; rather, it is established by proof. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument suggests that some deny that such knowledge is necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that if necessary knowledge can be based on theoretical, it loses its necessity entirely.
Correction: The proponents argue it does not lose necessity because the basis (knowledge of accidents) is not itself necessary; it is established by proof, so the final knowledge can still be necessary in a different sense.

Checkpoint

D) Because knowledge of opposites is purely intuitive.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school is divided on whether necessary knowledge can be grounded in theoretical knowledge, with the deniers emphasizing the independence of necessary knowledge and the permitters pointing to cases like the impossibility of opposites.

The Argument and the First Objection
Sanusi presents the argument and first objection-refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of the impossibility of opposites is used to argue that necessary knowledge may depend on theoretical knowledge, and why denying knowledge of the impossible is self-contradictory.

Core Idea
The denial of knowledge of the impossible is itself a form of knowledge.

This lesson begins with a classical argument: to know that two opposites cannot coexist, you must first know that opposites exist—something often learned through theoretical proof. But is that necessary knowledge itself dependent on theory? An objection arises: some say the impossible is not a thing and cannot be known. The author refutes this forcefully, showing that such denial is internally contradictory. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial argument
Read the passage from the beginning through the statement that necessary knowledge depends on theoretical knowledge. Notice how the example of opposites is used.
The argument assumes that knowing the existence of opposites is theoretical.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that the impossible cannot be known because it is not a thing. Locate this claim in the text.
3
Understand the refutation
The author calls the objector a stubborn disputant and a contradictor. See how the refutation shows that denying knowledge of the impossible entails knowing it.
The key point: even in denying knowability, one must conceive the impossibility.
4
Grasp the correct answer
The truth is that knowledge of the impossibility does not require the existence of the opposites; only their conception is needed.
This sets up the next lesson's discussion on the nature of conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الرابع عشر : هل يستند العلم الضروري إلى النظري
صح
الصفات المسماة بالأعراض مغايرة للذوات فمن لا يعلم وجود الأضداد كالسواد والبياض بذلك الدليل لم يحكم بامتناع الاجتماع بينها، فقد استناد الضروري إلى النظري ( ومن) أجاب عن هذا الاستدلال بأن منع العلم (به) أي بامتناع اجتماع الضدين بناء على أن العلم هو اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به والمستحيل ليس بشيء ( فهو مكابر) أي مانع مقتضى عقله (ومناقض لقوله ( فإن حكمه بعدم معلومية ذلك الامتناع يستلزم العلم به كما مر في أوائل الكتاب بل الحق) في الجواب عنه ( أنه ) أي العلم بامتناع اجتماع الضدين ( لا يتوقف على وجودهما) في الخارج، إذ لا توقف للتصديق على وجود أطرافه ( وأما تصورهما أي تصور الضدين (فنعم)
قوله : ( مغايرة للذوات) أي بحسب الوجود سواء كانت عين الذات كالمقدار فإنه عين الجواهر المنتظمة أو أموراً اعتبارية كالأعراض النسبية . قوله : ( ومنع العلم به أي بإنكار العلم به فضلاً عن كونه ضرورياً كما يرشد إليه دليله وقد صرح به المصنف في تعريف العلم حيث قال : ومن أنكر تعلق العلم بالمستحيل فهو مكابر ومناقض .
قوله : ( والمستحيل ليس بشيء فلا يتعلق به العلم به تصوراً ولا تصديقاً.
قوله : (مانع مقتضی عقله فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه الحكم باستحالة اجتماع الضدين ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع كون اجتماعهما معلوماً بوجه ما ؛ فتعلق به العلم التصديقي والتصوري . قوله : ( فإن حكمه بعدم إلخ ) كما ساق إليه دليله والتزم .
قوله : ( يستلزم العلم به أي تصوره إذ الحكم على المجهول المطلق محال فقد لزمه من الحكم عليه بعدم المعلومية معلوميته له تصديقاً ،وتصوراً فافهم فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام بعض الناظرين .
قوله : ( إذ لا توقف للتصديق أي لكل تصديق على وجود الأطراف بل إذا كان تصديقاً

قوله : ( بناء على أن العلم الخ فإن قلت : متعلق العلم فيما ذكر هو الامتناع لا المستحيل الذي هو الاجتماع، فبناء المنع على ما ذكره غير صحيح، قلت : العلم بامتناع الاجتماع لا يستلزم العلم باجتماع نفسه، وانتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم على أن الامتناع نفسه مستحيل أيضاً لأنه مما يمتنع وجوده في الخارج وإلا لزم وجود موضوعه فيه . قوله : ( فإن حكمه بعدم معلومية الخ) فيه مناقشة أن منع المعلومية هو المذكور فيما سبق، ومنعها ليس حكماً بعدمها وإلا كان المانع مدعياً فالأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : فإن منع معلوميته يستدعي تصوره، ويمكن أن يدفع بأن منع الشيء وإن لم يكن حكماً بعدمه مطلقاً إلا أن بناء المنع هاهنا على ما أشار إليه الشارح، بل المصنف أيضاً في صدر الكتاب يدل على أنهم يحكمون بانتفاء المعلومية، لكن يرد عليه أن مراد المانع منع التصديق بامتناع اجتماع الضدين لأنه الذي ادعى ضروريته فيما سبق، ويدل عليه قول الشارح أيضاً لم يحكم بامتناع الاجتماع فلا يتوقف العلم بذلك الامتناع عليه ) فإن التصديق الضروري هو ما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر وفكر وتوقفه على تصورهما مما لا شبهة فيه . ( ثم) إن قلت : تصور الضدين كالسواد والبياض نظري قطعاً فقد توقف ذلك التصديق الضروري المتعلق بامتناع اجتماعهما على علم نظري هو تصورهما قلت : ( إنه قد يكفي فيه ) أي في العلم بامتناع اجتماعهما ( تصورهما بوجه ما وقد يكون ذلك) القدر من التصور ( ضرورياً) فلا يكون حينئذ التصديق الضروري مستنداً إلى تصور نظري، ) فالحاصل أن هذا نزاع لفظي مرجعه إلى تفسير الضروري، فإن فسرنا التصديق الضروري بما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر كما مر جاز أن تكون مفرداته نظرية، وتوقف التصديق على النظر في مفرداته لا يقدح في استغناء حكمه عن نظر كاسب له في ذاته فيجوز استناد العلم الضروري إلى التصور النظري، وإن فسرناه بما لا يتوقف على نظر لا بذاته ولا بتوسط مفرداته لم يجز الاستناد المذكور، بل يكون مثل هذا التصديق غير ضروري، فإن عد نظرياً لزم اكتساب التصديقات النظرية من الأقوال الشارحة، وإلا كان واسطة بينهما ( وكذا توقفه أي توقف العلم الضروري ( على ضروري آخر) فيه خلاف راجع أيضاً إلى تفسير الضروري، ( فإن قلنا : هو ما لا يتوقف على علم سابق ( عليه (لم يجز) توقف الضروري على ضروري آخر ( وإن ما لا يتوقف على نظر (جاز) توقف الضروري على ضروري آخر فإن قلت :

إيجابياً خارجياً، وما نحن فيه في الحقيقة تصديق سلبي أي ليس بممكن الوجود في الخارج ولو سلم فهو تصديق ذهني يتوقف على تصور الأطراف وتميزه في العقل .

ينافيه تصور الامتناع وحمل العلم في قوله يستلزم العلم به على التصديق يدفعه قوله : كما مر في صدر الكتاب، كما يشهد به الرجوع إليه وإن كان له وجه في نفسه هذا، ويمكن أن يقال في وجه المناقضة أن منع العلم بامتناع الاجتماع تجويز ثلاجتماع ففيه تجويز لوجود الممتنع، وكل منع فيه تجويز لوجود الممتنع مناقض لنفسه لأن تجويزه شاهد على بطلانه فليتدبر. قوله : ( فالحاصل أن هذا الخ الأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : والحق أن هذا الخ لأن ما ذكره وإن كان كلاماً صحيحاً في نفسه إلا أن كونه حاصل ما ذكر أولاً ونتيجة له لا يخلو عن نوع تكلف كما لا يخفى على المتأمل في السياق .
Second type - Fourteenth topic: Does necessary knowledge depend on theoretical knowledge? Correct. Attributes named accidents are other than essences. So whoever does not know the existence of opposites like blackness and whiteness by that proof does not judge the impossibility of co-existence between them, so the necessary knowledge depends on theoretical knowledge. And someone answered this inference by denying knowledge of it, i.e., of the impossibility of co-existence of the two opposites, based on the fact that knowledge is belief in a thing as it is, and the impossible is not a thing, so he is a stubborn disputant, i.e., a preventer of his intellect's requirement, and contradictory to his statement, for his judgment of the non-knowability of that impossibility entails knowledge of it as has passed in the beginning of the book. Rather the truth in the answer about it is that the knowledge of the impossibility of co-existence of the two opposites does not depend on their existence in the external world, for there is no dependence of assent on the existence of its terms. As for their conception, i.e., the conception of the two opposites, yes. His statement: 'other than essences' means with respect to existence, whether they are identical to the essence like magnitude, for it is identical to well-ordered substances, or are mental considerations like relative accidents. His statement: 'and denying knowledge of it' i.e., by denying knowledge of it, let alone it being necessary, as his proof indicates, and the author explicitly stated in the definition of knowledge where he said: whoever denies the attachment of knowledge to the impossible is a stubborn disputant and a contradictor. His statement: 'and the impossible is not a thing, so knowledge does not attach to it, neither conception nor assent.' His statement: 'a preventer of his intellect's requirement' for every rational person finds in himself the judgment of the impossibility of co-existence of the two opposites, and does not conceive that except with their co-existence being known in some way; so the knowledge, both assent and conception, attaches to it. His statement: 'for his judgment of non-existence etc.' as he followed his proof and adhered to it. His statement: 'entails knowledge of it' i.e., its conception, for judgment of the absolutely unknown is impossible; so it follows from his judgment of it being non-knowable that it is known to him, both assent and conception. So understand, for some inquirers have slipped in this. His statement: 'for there is no dependence of assent' i.e., any assent, on the existence of the terms; rather if it is an assent... His statement: 'based on that knowledge etc.' For if you say: The object of knowledge in what is mentioned is the impossibility, not the impossible which is the co-existence, so the basis of the denial on what he mentioned is incorrect. I say: Knowledge of the impossibility of co-existence does not entail knowledge of the co-existence itself, and the non-existence of the consequent indicates the non-existence of the antecedent, especially since the impossibility itself is also impossible because it is something whose existence in the external world is impossible, otherwise the existence of its subject in it would be necessary. His statement: 'for his judgment of non-knowability etc.' In it there is a critique: that denial of knowability is what was mentioned previously, and denying it is not a judgment of its non-existence; otherwise the denier would be a claimant. So the most apparent in the expression is to say: For his denial of its knowability necessitates its conception. And it can be repelled by saying that denial of something, although not a judgment of its non-existence absolutely, but the basis of denial here is as the commentator indicated; rather the author also at the beginning of the book indicates that they judge the non-existence of knowability. But it is objected to him that the denier intends to deny the assent to the impossibility of co-existence of the two opposites because that which he claimed to be necessary previously, and the statement of the commentator also indicates that 'he did not judge the impossibility of co-existence, so the knowledge of that impossibility does not depend on it.' For the necessary assent is what does not depend after conception of the two terms on consideration and reflection, and its dependence on their conception is beyond doubt. (Then) If you say: The conception of the two opposites like blackness and whiteness is certainly theoretical, so that necessary assent related to the impossibility of their co-existence depends on theoretical knowledge which is their conception. I say: It may suffice in it, i.e., in the knowledge of the impossibility of their co-existence, their conception in some way, and that amount of conception may be necessary; then the necessary assent is not based on a theoretical conception. So the conclusion is that this is a verbal dispute whose reference is to the interpretation of the necessary. For if we interpret the necessary assent as what does not depend after conception of the two terms on consideration, as passed, it is permissible that its parts are theoretical, and the dependence of the assent on consideration in its parts does not detract from the independence of its judgment from a search that earns for it in itself, so it is permissible for the necessary knowledge to be based on theoretical conception. And if we interpret it as what does not depend on consideration neither in itself nor through its parts, then the aforementioned basing is not permissible; rather such an assent is not necessary. If it is counted theoretical, then the acquisition of theoretical assents from explanatory statements becomes necessary; otherwise it would be an intermediate between them. (And similarly) its dependence, i.e., the dependence of necessary knowledge, on another necessary knowledge: there is disagreement in it also referring to the interpretation of the necessary. For if we say: it is what does not depend on prior knowledge, the dependence of the necessary on another necessary is not permissible. And if we say: it is what does not depend on consideration, then the dependence of the necessary on another necessary is permissible. For if you say: We say: it is positively external, and what we are in actually is a negative assent, i.e., not possible of existence in the external; and if conceded, it is a mental assent that depends on the conception of the terms and its distinction in the mind. His statement: 'so the conclusion etc.' i.e., the conclusion from the inference and its answer: for from the inference it is taken that the necessary is what does not depend on consideration at all, and from the answer it is what does not depend after conception of the two terms on consideration, so it became a verbal dispute referring to the interpretation of the necessary. So what was said: the most apparent is to say 'and the truth' instead of 'so the conclusion' is not apparent. It is contradicted by the conception of impossibility and interpreting 'knowledge' in his statement 'entails knowledge of it' as assent is repelled by his statement 'as has passed in the beginning of the book', as is witnessed by returning to it, although it has a justification in itself. And it can be said in the aspect of contradiction that denying knowledge of the impossibility of co-existence is a permitting of co-existence, so in it there is a permitting of the existence of the impossible, and every denial that includes permitting the existence of the impossible is contradictory to itself because its permitting is a witness to its falsity, so reflect. His statement: 'so the conclusion that this etc.' the most apparent in the expression is to say: 'and the truth that this etc.' because what he mentioned, although it is correct speech in itself, but its being the conclusion of what was mentioned first and a result of it is not free from a sort of difficulty, as is not hidden from the one who reflects on the context.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of whether necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī) depends on theoretical knowledge (al-naẓarī). The author begins by presenting an argument: if someone does not know the existence of opposites (such as black and white) through a proof, they do not judge the impossibility of their co-existence. This shows that the necessary knowledge of that impossibility actually depends on theoretical knowledge (since knowing the existence of opposites is theoretical). Then an objection is raised: some might deny that we have knowledge of the impossibility of opposites, arguing that the impossible is not a thing and thus cannot be known. The author responds that such a denial is stubborn and self-contradictory, because even in denying its knowability, one implicitly knows it. The correct answer is that the knowledge of the impossibility does not depend on the existence of the opposites in the external world, because assent (taṣdīq) does not require the existence of its terms; rather, only their conception (taṣawwur) is needed. The passage then includes detailed commentary (sharḥ) explaining each phrase. The commentary clarifies that 'mukhālifa lil-dhāt' means the attributes (accidents) are other than essences in existence, whether they are identical like magnitude or mental like relative accidents. It explains that the denier is called a stubborn disputant because he opposes his own intellect's necessity. It also addresses a subtle point: the object of knowledge is the impossibility, not the impossible itself. The argument proceeds to a second objection: the conception of opposites (e.g., black and white) is itself theoretical, so the necessary knowledge would still depend on theoretical conception. The answer is that a sufficient conception may itself be necessary, so the necessary knowledge does not necessarily depend on theoretical conception. The author then concludes that this is a verbal dispute (nizā‘ lafẓī) about the definition of 'necessary knowledge'. If we define necessary assent as that which does not depend on consideration after conceiving the terms, then its parts may be theoretical and it can depend on theoretical conception. If we define it as that which depends on no consideration at all, then it cannot depend on theoretical conception. Similarly, the question of whether necessary knowledge depends on another necessary knowledge is also traced to the definition: if necessary means no prior knowledge, then it cannot; if it means no reflection, then it can. The passage ends with more commentary on the nature of the assent, noting that it is negative and mental, and the commentators discuss the best phrasing for the conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossible cannot be known in any way.
Correction: The passage shows that even in denying knowledge of the impossible, one has a conception of it, which is a form of knowledge. Moreover, the impossibility itself is known through assent (taṣdīq).

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the objector to be a stubborn disputant (mukābir)?
A) Because the objector denies knowledge of something that is self-evident.
B) Because the objector's denial of knowledge of the impossibility still entails having knowledge of it.
C) Because the objector fails to provide a proof.
D) Because the objector does not conceive the opposites.

Key Takeaway
Denying knowledge of the impossible is self-refuting because even the denial presupposes a conception of the impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth about Allah or His attributes because you thought it was impossible or beyond comprehension? How does this passage show that your very denial might actually prove that you already have some conception of it? What will you do about that hidden knowledge?
?
The objector claimed the impossible cannot be known. In your own life, have you ever used the excuse 'I can't know' to avoid grappling with a difficult truth about yourself or about Allah? What would change if you admitted that even your denial requires you to have conceived of that truth?
?
This passage distinguishes between needing proof from external existence and needing only conception. Your faith in the unseen—does it rely on seeing evidence or on conceiving the truth in your heart? Will you now commit to nurturing that conception rather than demanding visible signs?

Rational Judgments
advanced
10 min
The Verbal Dispute Over Necessary Knowledge
Sanusi presents the second objection, answer, and the conclusion that the dispute is verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection regarding the theoretical nature of the conception of opposites, the resolution that a sufficient conception may be necessary, and how the entire dispute reduces to a verbal disagreement about the definition of necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
The dispute over whether necessary knowledge depends on theory is a verbal one about definitions.

After establishing that knowledge of the impossibility of opposites does not require their existence, a new objection arises: the very conception of opposites (like black and white) is theoretical. So doesn't that make the necessary knowledge dependent on theoretical conception? The answer reveals a subtle point: a basic conception may itself be necessary. This leads the author to conclude that the entire debate is a verbal dispute about the meaning of 'necessary knowledge.'

Guided Path
1
Understand the second objection
The objector argues that the conception of opposites (e.g., black and white) is theoretical, so the necessary knowledge of their impossibility depends on theoretical conception.
This objection assumes that conception is always theoretical.
2
Follow the answer
The author responds that a sufficient conception may be necessary, not theoretical. Thus, necessary knowledge does not necessarily depend on theoretical conception.
3
See how this leads to a verbal dispute
The author concludes that the whole dispute is verbal, depending on how we define 'necessary knowledge'.
The two definitions are provided: (1) what does not depend on consideration after conceiving terms, and (2) what does not depend on consideration at all.
4
Examine the two definitions
Read the passage where the two interpretations are contrasted. Notice how each leads to a different conclusion about dependence on theoretical conception.
5
Apply to dependence on another necessary
The same reasoning is extended to whether necessary knowledge can depend on another necessary knowledge; it also reduces to the definition.

قوله : ( بناء على أن العلم الخ فإن قلت : متعلق العلم فيما ذكر هو الامتناع لا المستحيل الذي هو الاجتماع، فبناء المنع على ما ذكره غير صحيح، قلت : العلم بامتناع الاجتماع لا يستلزم العلم باجتماع نفسه، وانتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم على أن الامتناع نفسه مستحيل أيضاً لأنه مما يمتنع وجوده في الخارج وإلا لزم وجود موضوعه فيه . قوله : ( فإن حكمه بعدم معلومية الخ) فيه مناقشة أن منع المعلومية هو المذكور فيما سبق، ومنعها ليس حكماً بعدمها وإلا كان المانع مدعياً فالأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : فإن منع معلوميته يستدعي تصوره، ويمكن أن يدفع بأن منع الشيء وإن لم يكن حكماً بعدمه مطلقاً إلا أن بناء المنع هاهنا على ما أشار إليه الشارح، بل المصنف أيضاً في صدر الكتاب يدل على أنهم يحكمون بانتفاء المعلومية، لكن يرد عليه أن مراد المانع منع التصديق بامتناع اجتماع الضدين لأنه الذي ادعى ضروريته فيما سبق، ويدل عليه قول الشارح أيضاً لم يحكم بامتناع الاجتماع فلا يتوقف العلم بذلك الامتناع عليه ) فإن التصديق الضروري هو ما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر وفكر وتوقفه على تصورهما مما لا شبهة فيه . ( ثم) إن قلت : تصور الضدين كالسواد والبياض نظري قطعاً فقد توقف ذلك التصديق الضروري المتعلق بامتناع اجتماعهما على علم نظري هو تصورهما قلت : ( إنه قد يكفي فيه ) أي في العلم بامتناع اجتماعهما ( تصورهما بوجه ما وقد يكون ذلك) القدر من التصور ( ضرورياً) فلا يكون حينئذ التصديق الضروري مستنداً إلى تصور نظري، ) فالحاصل أن هذا نزاع لفظي مرجعه إلى تفسير الضروري، فإن فسرنا التصديق الضروري بما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر كما مر جاز أن تكون مفرداته نظرية، وتوقف التصديق على النظر في مفرداته لا يقدح في استغناء حكمه عن نظر كاسب له في ذاته فيجوز استناد العلم الضروري إلى التصور النظري، وإن فسرناه بما لا يتوقف على نظر لا بذاته ولا بتوسط مفرداته لم يجز الاستناد المذكور، بل يكون مثل هذا التصديق غير ضروري، فإن عد نظرياً لزم اكتساب التصديقات النظرية من الأقوال الشارحة، وإلا كان واسطة بينهما ( وكذا توقفه أي توقف العلم الضروري ( على ضروري آخر) فيه خلاف راجع أيضاً إلى تفسير الضروري، ( فإن قلنا : هو ما لا يتوقف على علم سابق ( عليه (لم يجز) توقف الضروري على ضروري آخر ( وإن ما لا يتوقف على نظر (جاز) توقف الضروري على ضروري آخر فإن قلت :
قلنا : هو
إيجابياً خارجياً، وما نحن فيه في الحقيقة تصديق سلبي أي ليس بممكن الوجود في الخارج ولو سلم فهو تصديق ذهني يتوقف على تصور الأطراف وتميزه في العقل .
قوله : ( فالحاصل إلخ) أي الحاصل من الاستدلال وجوابه فإنه قد استفيد من الاستدلال أن الضروري ما لا يتوقف على نظر أصلاً، ومن الجواب أنه ما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر فصار النزاع لفظياً راجعاً إلى تفسير الضروري فما قيل: الأظهر أن يقول: والحق بدل فالحاصل
ليس بظاهر .
ينافيه تصور الامتناع وحمل العلم في قوله يستلزم العلم به على التصديق يدفعه قوله : كما مر في صدر الكتاب، كما يشهد به الرجوع إليه وإن كان له وجه في نفسه هذا، ويمكن أن يقال في وجه المناقضة أن منع العلم بامتناع الاجتماع تجويز ثلاجتماع ففيه تجويز لوجود الممتنع، وكل منع فيه تجويز لوجود الممتنع مناقض لنفسه لأن تجويزه شاهد على بطلانه فليتدبر. قوله : ( فالحاصل أن هذا الخ الأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : والحق أن هذا الخ لأن ما ذكره وإن كان كلاماً صحيحاً في نفسه إلا أن كونه حاصل ما ذكر أولاً ونتيجة له لا يخلو عن نوع تكلف كما لا يخفى على المتأمل في السياق .
Second type - Fourteenth topic: Does necessary knowledge depend on theoretical knowledge? Correct. Attributes named accidents are other than essences. So whoever does not know the existence of opposites like blackness and whiteness by that proof does not judge the impossibility of co-existence between them, so the necessary knowledge depends on theoretical knowledge. And someone answered this inference by denying knowledge of it, i.e., of the impossibility of co-existence of the two opposites, based on the fact that knowledge is belief in a thing as it is, and the impossible is not a thing, so he is a stubborn disputant, i.e., a preventer of his intellect's requirement, and contradictory to his statement, for his judgment of the non-knowability of that impossibility entails knowledge of it as has passed in the beginning of the book. Rather the truth in the answer about it is that the knowledge of the impossibility of co-existence of the two opposites does not depend on their existence in the external world, for there is no dependence of assent on the existence of its terms. As for their conception, i.e., the conception of the two opposites, yes. His statement: 'other than essences' means with respect to existence, whether they are identical to the essence like magnitude, for it is identical to well-ordered substances, or are mental considerations like relative accidents. His statement: 'and denying knowledge of it' i.e., by denying knowledge of it, let alone it being necessary, as his proof indicates, and the author explicitly stated in the definition of knowledge where he said: whoever denies the attachment of knowledge to the impossible is a stubborn disputant and a contradictor. His statement: 'and the impossible is not a thing, so knowledge does not attach to it, neither conception nor assent.' His statement: 'a preventer of his intellect's requirement' for every rational person finds in himself the judgment of the impossibility of co-existence of the two opposites, and does not conceive that except with their co-existence being known in some way; so the knowledge, both assent and conception, attaches to it. His statement: 'for his judgment of non-existence etc.' as he followed his proof and adhered to it. His statement: 'entails knowledge of it' i.e., its conception, for judgment of the absolutely unknown is impossible; so it follows from his judgment of it being non-knowable that it is known to him, both assent and conception. So understand, for some inquirers have slipped in this. His statement: 'for there is no dependence of assent' i.e., any assent, on the existence of the terms; rather if it is an assent... His statement: 'based on that knowledge etc.' For if you say: The object of knowledge in what is mentioned is the impossibility, not the impossible which is the co-existence, so the basis of the denial on what he mentioned is incorrect. I say: Knowledge of the impossibility of co-existence does not entail knowledge of the co-existence itself, and the non-existence of the consequent indicates the non-existence of the antecedent, especially since the impossibility itself is also impossible because it is something whose existence in the external world is impossible, otherwise the existence of its subject in it would be necessary. His statement: 'for his judgment of non-knowability etc.' In it there is a critique: that denial of knowability is what was mentioned previously, and denying it is not a judgment of its non-existence; otherwise the denier would be a claimant. So the most apparent in the expression is to say: For his denial of its knowability necessitates its conception. And it can be repelled by saying that denial of something, although not a judgment of its non-existence absolutely, but the basis of denial here is as the commentator indicated; rather the author also at the beginning of the book indicates that they judge the non-existence of knowability. But it is objected to him that the denier intends to deny the assent to the impossibility of co-existence of the two opposites because that which he claimed to be necessary previously, and the statement of the commentator also indicates that 'he did not judge the impossibility of co-existence, so the knowledge of that impossibility does not depend on it.' For the necessary assent is what does not depend after conception of the two terms on consideration and reflection, and its dependence on their conception is beyond doubt. (Then) If you say: The conception of the two opposites like blackness and whiteness is certainly theoretical, so that necessary assent related to the impossibility of their co-existence depends on theoretical knowledge which is their conception. I say: It may suffice in it, i.e., in the knowledge of the impossibility of their co-existence, their conception in some way, and that amount of conception may be necessary; then the necessary assent is not based on a theoretical conception. So the conclusion is that this is a verbal dispute whose reference is to the interpretation of the necessary. For if we interpret the necessary assent as what does not depend after conception of the two terms on consideration, as passed, it is permissible that its parts are theoretical, and the dependence of the assent on consideration in its parts does not detract from the independence of its judgment from a search that earns for it in itself, so it is permissible for the necessary knowledge to be based on theoretical conception. And if we interpret it as what does not depend on consideration neither in itself nor through its parts, then the aforementioned basing is not permissible; rather such an assent is not necessary. If it is counted theoretical, then the acquisition of theoretical assents from explanatory statements becomes necessary; otherwise it would be an intermediate between them. (And similarly) its dependence, i.e., the dependence of necessary knowledge, on another necessary knowledge: there is disagreement in it also referring to the interpretation of the necessary. For if we say: it is what does not depend on prior knowledge, the dependence of the necessary on another necessary is not permissible. And if we say: it is what does not depend on consideration, then the dependence of the necessary on another necessary is permissible. For if you say: We say: it is positively external, and what we are in actually is a negative assent, i.e., not possible of existence in the external; and if conceded, it is a mental assent that depends on the conception of the terms and its distinction in the mind. His statement: 'so the conclusion etc.' i.e., the conclusion from the inference and its answer: for from the inference it is taken that the necessary is what does not depend on consideration at all, and from the answer it is what does not depend after conception of the two terms on consideration, so it became a verbal dispute referring to the interpretation of the necessary. So what was said: the most apparent is to say 'and the truth' instead of 'so the conclusion' is not apparent. It is contradicted by the conception of impossibility and interpreting 'knowledge' in his statement 'entails knowledge of it' as assent is repelled by his statement 'as has passed in the beginning of the book', as is witnessed by returning to it, although it has a justification in itself. And it can be said in the aspect of contradiction that denying knowledge of the impossibility of co-existence is a permitting of co-existence, so in it there is a permitting of the existence of the impossible, and every denial that includes permitting the existence of the impossible is contradictory to itself because its permitting is a witness to its falsity, so reflect. His statement: 'so the conclusion that this etc.' the most apparent in the expression is to say: 'and the truth that this etc.' because what he mentioned, although it is correct speech in itself, but its being the conclusion of what was mentioned first and a result of it is not free from a sort of difficulty, as is not hidden from the one who reflects on the context.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of whether necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī) depends on theoretical knowledge (al-naẓarī). The author begins by presenting an argument: if someone does not know the existence of opposites (such as black and white) through a proof, they do not judge the impossibility of their co-existence. This shows that the necessary knowledge of that impossibility actually depends on theoretical knowledge (since knowing the existence of opposites is theoretical). Then an objection is raised: some might deny that we have knowledge of the impossibility of opposites, arguing that the impossible is not a thing and thus cannot be known. The author responds that such a denial is stubborn and self-contradictory, because even in denying its knowability, one implicitly knows it. The correct answer is that the knowledge of the impossibility does not depend on the existence of the opposites in the external world, because assent (taṣdīq) does not require the existence of its terms; rather, only their conception (taṣawwur) is needed. The passage then includes detailed commentary (sharḥ) explaining each phrase. The commentary clarifies that 'mukhālifa lil-dhāt' means the attributes (accidents) are other than essences in existence, whether they are identical like magnitude or mental like relative accidents. It explains that the denier is called a stubborn disputant because he opposes his own intellect's necessity. It also addresses a subtle point: the object of knowledge is the impossibility, not the impossible itself. The argument proceeds to a second objection: the conception of opposites (e.g., black and white) is itself theoretical, so the necessary knowledge would still depend on theoretical conception. The answer is that a sufficient conception may itself be necessary, so the necessary knowledge does not necessarily depend on theoretical conception. The author then concludes that this is a verbal dispute (nizā‘ lafẓī) about the definition of 'necessary knowledge'. If we define necessary assent as that which does not depend on consideration after conceiving the terms, then its parts may be theoretical and it can depend on theoretical conception. If we define it as that which depends on no consideration at all, then it cannot depend on theoretical conception. Similarly, the question of whether necessary knowledge depends on another necessary knowledge is also traced to the definition: if necessary means no prior knowledge, then it cannot; if it means no reflection, then it can. The passage ends with more commentary on the nature of the assent, noting that it is negative and mental, and the commentators discuss the best phrasing for the conclusion.
