Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge can never depend on theoretical knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that depending on the definition, necessary knowledge may or may not depend on theoretical knowledge; it is a verbal dispute, not a substantive one.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ultimate nature of the dispute about whether necessary knowledge depends on theoretical knowledge?
A) It is a substantive disagreement about the nature of knowledge.
B) It is a verbal dispute that reduces to the definition of 'necessary knowledge'.
C) It is settled by the argument from the impossibility of opposites.
D) It depends on whether the conception of opposites is theoretical.

Key Takeaway
The question of whether necessary knowledge depends on theoretical knowledge is a verbal dispute, resolved by clarifying the definition of necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a disagreement about Islamic beliefs, do you first clarify the definitions of the key terms involved? This lesson shows that many disputes are merely verbal. How often do you assume a real disagreement when it might just be a matter of definition? Will you commit to defining terms before arguing next time?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you consider 'necessarily true' about Allah. On what is that certainty based? Is it built on definitions you have accepted or on direct, undeniable experience? This passage challenges you to examine the foundation of your most certain convictions. Are you willing to re-examine them in light of this distinction?
?
The passage discusses that necessary knowledge can have theoretical parts. Do you have areas in your faith where you rely on theological reasoning but then treat the conclusions as unquestionable? Could it be that you are confusing a necessary judgment with a theoretical one? This lesson invites you to honestly assess the source of your religious certitudes.

The Verbal Dispute on Necessary Knowledge's Dependence
Al-Jurjani analyzes the verbal nature of the dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dispute over whether necessary knowledge depends on theoretical knowledge is resolved as a verbal dispute based on the definition of 'necessary'.

Core Idea
The dispute is verbal, turning on how we define 'necessary knowledge'.

This passage tackles a subtle question: can necessary knowledge—knowledge that seems immediate—depend on theoretical reasoning? Al-Jurjani shows that the apparent disagreement is not substantive but verbal, rooted in differing definitions of 'necessary'. By carefully examining the definitions, we see that both sides can be reconciled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
Read the passage's opening to understand the question: Does necessary knowledge depend on theoretical knowledge? Notice the example about impossibility of opposites.
The example of blackness and whiteness frames the debate.
2
Trace the objection and answer
Find the objection: conception of opposites is theoretical, so necessary judgment would depend on theory. Then see the answer: a minimal conception may be necessary.
This exchange shows the core of the dispute.
3
Understand the resolution
The passage concludes that the dispute is verbal, depending on how 'necessary' is defined. Two definitions are compared.
Focus on the two definitions and their consequences.
4
Extend the reasoning to another case
The same logic applies to necessary knowledge depending on another necessary knowledge. See how the definition again determines the answer.
This reinforces the pattern.

النوع الثاني - المقصد الرابع عشر : هل يستند العلم الضروري إلى النظري
يتوقف العلم بذلك الامتناع عليه ) فإن التصديق الضروري هو ما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر وفكر وتوقفه على تصورهما مما لا شبهة فيه . ( ثم) إن قلت : تصور الضدين كالسواد والبياض نظري قطعاً فقد توقف ذلك التصديق الضروري المتعلق بامتناع اجتماعهما على علم نظري هو تصورهما قلت : ( إنه قد يكفي فيه ) أي في العلم بامتناع اجتماعهما ( تصورهما بوجه ما وقد يكون ذلك) القدر من التصور ( ضرورياً) فلا يكون حينئذ التصديق الضروري مستنداً إلى تصور نظري، ) فالحاصل أن هذا نزاع لفظي مرجعه إلى تفسير الضروري، فإن فسرنا التصديق الضروري بما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر كما مر جاز أن تكون مفرداته نظرية، وتوقف التصديق على النظر في مفرداته لا يقدح في استغناء حكمه عن نظر كاسب له في ذاته فيجوز استناد العلم الضروري إلى التصور النظري، وإن فسرناه بما لا يتوقف على نظر لا بذاته ولا بتوسط مفرداته لم يجز الاستناد المذكور، بل يكون مثل هذا التصديق غير ضروري، فإن عد نظرياً لزم اكتساب التصديقات النظرية من الأقوال الشارحة، وإلا كان واسطة بينهما ( وكذا توقفه أي توقف العلم الضروري ( على ضروري آخر) فيه خلاف راجع أيضاً إلى تفسير الضروري، ( فإن قلنا : هو ما لا يتوقف على علم سابق ( عليه (لم يجز) توقف الضروري على ضروري آخر ( وإن ما لا يتوقف على نظر (جاز) توقف الضروري على ضروري آخر فإن قلت : قلنا : هو إيجابياً خارجياً، وما نحن فيه في الحقيقة تصديق سلبي أي ليس بممكن الوجود في الخارج ولو سلم فهو تصديق ذهني يتوقف على تصور الأطراف وتميزه في العقل .
قوله : ( فالحاصل إلخ) أي الحاصل من الاستدلال وجوابه فإنه قد استفيد من الاستدلال أن الضروري ما لا يتوقف على نظر أصلاً، ومن الجواب أنه ما لا يتوقف بعد تصور الطرفين على نظر فصار النزاع لفظياً راجعاً إلى تفسير الضروري فما قيل: الأظهر أن يقول: والحق بدل فالحاصل ليس بظاهر .
ينافيه تصور الامتناع وحمل العلم في قوله يستلزم العلم به على التصديق يدفعه قوله : كما مر في صدر الكتاب، كما يشهد به الرجوع إليه وإن كان له وجه في نفسه هذا، ويمكن أن يقال في وجه المناقضة أن منع العلم بامتناع الاجتماع تجويز ثلاجتماع ففيه تجويز لوجود الممتنع، وكل منع فيه تجويز لوجود الممتنع مناقض لنفسه لأن تجويزه شاهد على بطلانه فليتدبر. قوله : ( فالحاصل أن هذا الخ الأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : والحق أن هذا الخ لأن ما ذكره وإن كان كلاماً صحيحاً في نفسه إلا أن كونه حاصل ما ذكر أولاً ونتيجة له لا يخلو عن نوع تكلف كما لا يخفى على المتأمل في السياق .
The second type - the fourteenth objective: Does necessary knowledge depend on theoretical knowledge? Knowledge of that impossibility depends on it, for the necessary judgment is that which, after conceiving the two extremes, does not depend on reflection and thought, and its dependence on their conception is indisputable. Then if you say: Conception of two opposites, like blackness and whiteness, is definitely theoretical; thus that necessary judgment concerning the impossibility of their coexistence depends on a theoretical knowledge, which is their conception, I say: It may suffice in that (i.e., in knowledge of the impossibility of their coexistence) their conception in some manner, and that amount of conception may be necessary; then the necessary judgment would not be based on a theoretical conception. So the conclusion is that this is a verbal dispute whose reference is to the interpretation of 'necessary'. For if we interpret necessary judgment as that which, after conceiving the two extremes, does not depend on reflection, as previously, then its terms may be theoretical, and the judgment's dependence on reflection on its terms does not detract from its ruling being independent of inferential reflection in itself; thus it is permissible for necessary knowledge to be based on theoretical conception. But if we interpret it as that which does not depend on reflection, neither essentially nor through its terms, then the aforementioned basis is not permissible; rather such a judgment would not be necessary. If it is considered theoretical, then theoretical judgments would necessarily be acquired from explicative statements; otherwise it would be an intermediary between them. Similarly, its dependence (i.e., the dependence of necessary knowledge) on another necessary (knowledge) has a dispute that also refers to the interpretation of 'necessary'. For if we say it is that which does not depend on prior knowledge, then the dependence of the necessary on another necessary is not permissible. But if we say it is that which does not depend on reflection, then the dependence of the necessary on another necessary is permissible. For if you say: We say: It is an external affirmative, and what we are discussing is in reality a negative judgment, i.e., not possible of existence externally; and even if it is granted, it is a mental judgment that depends on the conception of the terms and their distinction in the intellect. His saying: (Thus the conclusion, etc.) meaning the conclusion of the argument and its answer, for it has been derived from the argument that the necessary is that which does not depend on reflection at all, and from the answer that it is that which after conceiving the two extremes does not depend on reflection; thus the dispute became verbal, referring to the interpretation of 'necessary'. So what was said: It is more apparent that he should say: The truth, instead of 'thus the conclusion', is not apparent. The conception of impossibility contradicts it, and applying 'knowledge' in his saying 'knowledge of it entails' to judgment is refuted by his saying: as previously in the beginning of the book, as verified by referring to it, even if it has a face in itself. And it can be said in the manner of contradiction that forbidding knowledge of the impossibility of coexistence is to permit coexistence, and in it is a permission for the existence of the impossible, and every forbiddance that contains permission for the existence of the impossible contradicts itself because its permission testifies to its falsity. So reflect. His saying: (Thus the conclusion that this, etc.) The more apparent in the expression is to say: The truth is that this, etc., because what he mentioned, even if it is correct in itself, yet being the conclusion of what was mentioned first and its result is not without some contrivance, as is not hidden from the one who reflects on the context.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines and resolves the verbal dispute about whether necessary knowledge can depend on theoretical knowledge. The author (al-Iji) presents the definition of necessary judgment as that which does not depend on reflection after conceptualization. An objection is raised: the conception of opposites is theoretical, so the necessary judgment of their incompatibility would depend on theory. The answer clarifies that a minimal conceptualization may be necessary. The conclusion (fā’l-ḥāṣil) is that the dispute is verbal, turning on the definition of 'necessary'. If necessary means 'not depending on reflection after conceptualization', then its terms can be theoretical and the judgment's independence is preserved. If it means 'not depending on reflection at all', then such dependence ruins necessity. The same applies to dependence on another necessary judgment. The commentator then explains the derivation of the conclusion from the argument and answer, and addresses a criticism about using 'fā’l-ḥāṣil' instead of 'al-ḥaqq'. He also discusses a possible contradiction in the objection and suggests a deeper reflection.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any dependence on theoretical conception destroys the necessity of a judgment.
Correction: The passage shows that if 'necessary' is defined as not depending on reflection after conceptualization, then the terms can be theoretical as long as the judgment itself does not require inferential reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a necessary judgment remain necessary even if its terms are theoretical?
A) When the judgment is about external objects.
B) When 'necessary' is defined as not depending on reflection after conceptualization.
C) When the judgment is negative.
D) When the terms are conceived necessarily.

Key Takeaway
The dispute about necessary knowledge depending on theoretical knowledge is resolved as verbal, turning on the definition of 'necessary'.

Clarifying 'Prior Knowledge' in Necessary Judgments
Al-Jurjani clarifies the meaning of 'prior knowledge' in necessary judgment definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what 'prior knowledge' means in the definition of necessary judgment and why necessary judgments can depend on prior conceptions.

Core Idea
Necessary judgments depend on prior conceptions, not prior judgments.

Al-Jurjani addresses an apparent contradiction: necessary judgments are defined as not depending on prior knowledge, yet they clearly depend on conceptions. How can both be true? He explains that 'prior knowledge' in the definition refers specifically to prior judgments, not all prior knowledge. This resolution preserves the definition while accounting for the foundational role of conceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words guide you.
2
Identify the problem
Necessary judgments depend on necessary conceptions. But the definition says they do not depend on prior knowledge. This seems contradictory.
3
Find the answer
The answer: 'prior knowledge' here means prior judgment, not prior conception. So necessary judgments can depend on conceptions without violating the definition.
This is the key insight.
4
Understand the extension
Al-Jurjani then applies this to two interpretations of 'necessary' – one as not depending on prior knowledge, the other as not depending on reflection. Each has different implications for theoretical terms.
Notice how the same distinction resolves both disputes.
5
Reflect on importance
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter? It clarifies what 'prior knowledge' excludes and what it allows, preserving the coherence of the classification of judgments.

التصديقات الضرورية موقوفة على تصورات أطرافها الضرورية بلا نزاع، فكيف يفسر الضروري بما لا يتوقف على علم سابق ؟ قلت : المراد بالعلم السابق هو التصديق ولك أن تجعل قوله : فإن قلنا إلى آخره مرجعاً للنزاعين معاً، فإن الضروري المفسر بما لا يتوقف على علم سابق لا يجوز توقفه على ضروري آخر ولا على نظري أيضاً، والمفسر بما لا يتوقف على نظر يتناول التصديق الضروري الذي تكون مفرداته نظرية إذا أريد أنه لا يتوقف على نظر يتضمنه أو يكون كاسباً له بالذات .
Necessary judgments are contingent on the necessary conceptions of their terms without dispute. So how is 'necessary' interpreted as that which does not depend on any prior knowledge? I say: What is meant by 'prior knowledge' is the judgment. And you may take his saying: 'for if we say' to the end as a reference for both disputes together. For the necessary interpreted as that which does not depend on prior knowledge does not allow its dependence on another necessary nor on the theoretical either. But the necessary interpreted as that which does not depend on reflection includes the necessary judgment whose terms are theoretical, if it is intended that it does not depend on reflection that is inherent in it or that is acquisitive for it essentially.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage clarifies the meaning of 'prior knowledge' in the definition of necessary judgment. The commentator begins by stating that necessary judgments undoubtedly depend on the necessary conceptions of their terms. A question arises: if necessary judgments depend on such conceptions, how can 'necessary' be defined as that which does not depend on any prior knowledge? The answer: 'prior knowledge' here specifically means a prior judgment, not a prior conception. This interpretation resolves the apparent contradiction. The commentator then notes that this interpretive distinction also applies to the two disputes mentioned earlier (dependence on another necessary and dependence on theoretical). If 'necessary' is defined as not depending on any prior knowledge at all, then it cannot depend on another necessary nor on a theoretical knowledge. But if defined as not depending on reflection, then it accommodates necessary judgments whose terms are theoretical, as long as the judgment itself does not depend on its own internal reflection or acquisitive reflection.

Key Takeaway
The definition of necessary judgment uses 'prior knowledge' to mean prior judgment, allowing necessary judgments to depend on prior conceptions.

Clarifying Necessary Knowledge (التصديق الضروري)
Clarifies the definition of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessary knowledge is defined as not depending on prior theoretical knowledge, and why prior affirmation is not included.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge requires only prior concepts, not prior affirmations.

The passage begins by addressing a potential paradox: necessary affirmations (التصديقات الضرورية) depend on necessary concepts of their terms. This might seem to imply that necessary knowledge requires prior knowledge. The author clarifies that 'prior knowledge' in the definition means prior affirmation (تصديق), not prior conception (تصور), thus resolving the paradox.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening question
The passage opens with a question: if necessary affirmations depend on necessary concepts, how can the necessary be defined as that which does not depend on prior knowledge?
Notice the tension: dependence on concepts seems to contradict the definition.
2
Understand the author's clarification
The author explains that 'prior knowledge' means prior affirmation. Necessary affirmations may depend on prior concepts, but that does not violate the definition.
This distinction is crucial for the rest of the discussion.
3
Examine the two interpretations
The author provides two ways to interpret the definition of the necessary: one that excludes both other necessary and theoretical knowledge, and another that only excludes theoretical knowledge.
These interpretations affect which affirmations count as necessary.

The Second Type - The Fifteenth Investigation: Knowledge with no Known Object. Necessary affirmations depend on necessary concepts of their terms without dispute, so how can the necessary be defined as that which does not depend on prior knowledge? I say: By prior knowledge is meant affirmation, and you may take his statement: 'for if we say' etc. as a reference for both disputes. For the necessary defined as that which does not depend on prior knowledge cannot depend on another necessary nor on theoretical [knowledge] either. And the defined as that which does not depend on theoretical [knowledge] includes the necessary affirmation whose terms are theoretical if it is intended that it does not depend on theoretical [knowledge] that it includes or that acquires it essentially. [The Fifteenth Investigation: Knowledge with no Known Object] Abu Hashim affirmed knowledge with no known object, like knowledge of the impossible. For it—i.e. the impossible—is not a thing, and the known is a thing, so here there is knowledge with no known object. And the rational people have agreed on the impossibility of knowledge with no known object. Imam al-Razi said: It is a contradiction, for 'the known' means nothing other than that to which knowledge is attached. If it is said: The impossible has knowledge attached to it and it is not known, that is equivalent to saying: The impossible is an object of knowledge and is not an object of it. Al-Amidi said: He may stipulate that he does not call it 'known'—i.e., he may stipulate that the object of knowledge, if it is impossible, he does not call it 'known', and if it is not impossible, he calls it 'known'. But this stipulation has no benefit. The author said: The fair-minded position is that you should not suspect a word that comes out of your brother's mouth of evil—i.e., error—and seek for it—i.e., what comes out—a valid interpretation as much as you can. Rather, interpret his words—i.e., the words of Abu Hashim that are transmitted from him—in accordance with what Ibn Sina clarified: that the impossible does not have a form in the mind—i.e., we have no way to perceive it in itself such that a form of it in its particularity obtains in the mind. So it is impossible to conceive something that is the conjunction of contradictories or opposites. So its conception—i.e., the conception of the impossible—is either... from the Shifa. His statement: (al-su' with damm is the object of la tazunn, and al-zann means accusation.) His statement: (it is a conjunction upon 'an la tazunn': in some copies without the word 'an, which is apparent, and in most copies with the word 'an, which is a slip of the scribe's pen, having no justification except that it is said: the conjunction is upon 'la tazunn' in 'an la tazunn' because it is clear that conjunction upon the entire phrase is not correct, and he chose this expression due to the ugliness of separating the relative from the clause.) His statement: (that the impossible etc. because the mental form exists externally as one of the psychological qualities and is united with the known. If the impossible had a particular form in the mind, its quiddity would exist externally with original existence to have effects upon it, and at least the person would be knowledgeable of it. Then its quiddity would not necessitate the impossibility of existence. This is like the Necessary [Existent] which has no particular form in the mind, otherwise its quiddity would be established by another in the outside. Unlike the possible non-existent, for the obtaining of its particular form in the mind only requires that it have original existence in terms of its subsistence in the intellect, and it has no existence outside the mind, and there is no problem in that. His statement: 'so its conception' i.e. the conception of the impossible etc. in a chapter of the first article of the fifth [book] of The Logic of al-Shifa. And here there is a doubt: How can the non-existent whose essence is impossible of existence be conceived when asked about it as to what it is, so that after that one seeks whether it is [existent] or not? For if no meaning of it obtains in the mind, how can it be conceived as existent or non-existent? And the impossible has no form in existence, so how can a form of it exist in the mind such that the conceived thing is its meaning? We respond to this: This impossible is either simple with no composition... His statement: (the conjunction upon 'an la tazunn': it is not hidden that it is a conjunction upon 'la tazunn', and he only brought the word 'an in the conjoined upon to indicate by the use of the accusative operator that it is accusative.)
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument regarding the possibility of knowledge without a known object (علم لا معلوم له), a thesis attributed to Abu Hashim. The author begins with a preliminary discussion on necessary knowledge (التصديق الضروري), clarifying that 'prior knowledge' (العلم السابق) in the definition of necessary knowledge means prior affirmation (تصديق), not prior conception. This is necessary to avoid the paradox that necessary affirmations would depend on prior necessary concepts, which would make them non-necessary. The author then turns to the main topic: Abu Hashim's claim that there is knowledge of the impossible (المستحيل). Since the impossible is 'not a thing' (ليس بشيء) and the known (المعلوم) is defined as a thing, knowledge of the impossible would be knowledge without a known object. Al-Razi objects that this is contradictory, as 'the known' is precisely that to which knowledge is attached. Al-Amidi suggests a terminological solution: one may simply refuse to call the impossible 'known', but the author deems this useless. The author provides the resolution by invoking Ibn Sina's doctrine that the impossible has no form (صورة) in the mind. That is, we cannot conceive the impossible in its particular essence; rather, we conceive it in a secondary way, e.g., as a composition of incompatible attributes. The long gloss that follows elaborates on this: if the impossible had a specific mental form, its quiddity would have to exist externally, which is false. This is compared to the Necessary Existent (الواجب), which also has no particular form in the mind. The passage ends with a doubt (شك) about how the impossible can be conceived at all, and the beginning of an answer distinguishing simple and composite impossibles. The text is cut off mid-sentence.

العلم السابق
التصور
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That necessary knowledge requires no prior knowledge of any kind, including concepts.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'prior knowledge' in the definition means prior affirmation, not prior conception. Necessary knowledge can depend on concepts.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'prior knowledge' (العلم السابق) in the definition of necessary knowledge refer to?
A) Prior conception (التصور)
B) Prior affirmation (التصديق)
C) Both conception and affirmation
D) Neither conception nor affirmation

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge is defined as not depending on prior affirmation, but it may depend on prior concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have beliefs you consider absolutely certain, like that 2+2=4 or that God exists. If these depend on concepts (like numbers or 'God'), are you absolutely certain that those concepts are correct? Could your certainty be misplaced if your concepts are flawed?
?
Consider a foundational belief in your faith that you hold to be necessary knowledge. Has your belief ever been challenged by a more refined understanding of the concepts involved? How do you respond when you realize a 'necessary' truth actually depends on a concept you never examined?
?
The lesson says necessary knowledge depends on necessary concepts. What if one of those concepts is actually confused or false? Would your 'necessary' belief remain necessary? If not, what does that mean for the foundations of your certainty?

Abu Hashim's Claim and the Objections
Presents the problem of knowledge without a known object and initial objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument for knowledge without a known object, and the objections raised by al-Razi and al-Amidi.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the impossible seems to be knowledge without an object, but it is impossible by consensus.

This lesson presents the core problem of the fifteenth investigation: Abu Hashim claimed that knowledge of the impossible (المستحيل) is knowledge with no known object (علم لا معلوم له), because the impossible is not a thing while the known is a thing. Al-Razi and al-Amidi each raise objections, but the author will later provide a resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hashim's claim
Abu Hashim argues that since the impossible is not a thing, and the known is a thing, knowledge of the impossible has no known object.
This is the thesis being debated.
2
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi says this is a contradiction: 'the known' just means what knowledge is attached to, so if knowledge is attached to the impossible, it is by definition known.
Al-Razi's point is that the phrase 'knowledge without a known object' is self-contradictory.
3
Examine al-Amidi's terminological solution
Al-Amidi suggests one could stipulate not to call the impossible 'known', but the author dismisses this as useless.
This solution does not address the substantive issue.

قوله : ( إن المستحيل إلخ لأن الصورة العقلية موجودة خارجي من الكيفيات النفسانية ومتحدة بالمعلوم، فلو كان للمستحيل بخصوصه صورة في العقل لكان ماهيته موجودة في الخارج بالوجود الأصيلي ليترتب الآثار عليه ولا أقل من كون الشخص عالماً به، فلا يكون ماهيته مقتضية لامتناع الوجود وهذا كالواجب ليس له بخصوصه صورة في العقل، وإلا لزم قيام ماهيته بالغير في الخارج بخلاف المعدوم الممكن فإن حصول صورته المخصوصة في العقل، إنما يقتضي أن يكون له وجودا أصيلي من حيث قيامه بالذهن ولا يكون له وجود في خارج الذهن ولا
محذور فيه .
قوله: فتصوره أي تصور المستحيل إلخ في فصل من المقالة الأولى من الخامس من ( منطق الشفاء، وهاهنا موضع شك في أن المعدوم الذات المحال الوجود كيف يتصور إذا سئل عنه بما هو حتى يطلب بعد ذلك، هل هو فإنه إن لم يحصل له في العقل معنى كيف يتصور بأنه حاصل أو غير حاصل والمحال لا صورة له في الوجود، فكيف يوجد عنه صورة في الذهن يكون ذلك المتصور معناه ؟ فنقول في جوابه : إن هذا المحال إما أن يكون مفرداً لا تركيب فيه ولا
قوله : ( عطف على أن لا تظن لا يخفى أنه عطف على لا تظن وإنما أورد لفظه أن في
المعطوف عليه ليعلم بإيراد عامل النصب أنه منصوب .
The Second Type - The Fifteenth Investigation: Knowledge with no Known Object. Necessary affirmations depend on necessary concepts of their terms without dispute, so how can the necessary be defined as that which does not depend on prior knowledge? I say: By prior knowledge is meant affirmation, and you may take his statement: 'for if we say' etc. as a reference for both disputes. For the necessary defined as that which does not depend on prior knowledge cannot depend on another necessary nor on theoretical [knowledge] either. And the defined as that which does not depend on theoretical [knowledge] includes the necessary affirmation whose terms are theoretical if it is intended that it does not depend on theoretical [knowledge] that it includes or that acquires it essentially. [The Fifteenth Investigation: Knowledge with no Known Object] Abu Hashim affirmed knowledge with no known object, like knowledge of the impossible. For it—i.e. the impossible—is not a thing, and the known is a thing, so here there is knowledge with no known object. And the rational people have agreed on the impossibility of knowledge with no known object. Imam al-Razi said: It is a contradiction, for 'the known' means nothing other than that to which knowledge is attached. If it is said: The impossible has knowledge attached to it and it is not known, that is equivalent to saying: The impossible is an object of knowledge and is not an object of it. Al-Amidi said: He may stipulate that he does not call it 'known'—i.e., he may stipulate that the object of knowledge, if it is impossible, he does not call it 'known', and if it is not impossible, he calls it 'known'. But this stipulation has no benefit. The author said: The fair-minded position is that you should not suspect a word that comes out of your brother's mouth of evil—i.e., error—and seek for it—i.e., what comes out—a valid interpretation as much as you can. Rather, interpret his words—i.e., the words of Abu Hashim that are transmitted from him—in accordance with what Ibn Sina clarified: that the impossible does not have a form in the mind—i.e., we have no way to perceive it in itself such that a form of it in its particularity obtains in the mind. So it is impossible to conceive something that is the conjunction of contradictories or opposites. So its conception—i.e., the conception of the impossible—is either... from the Shifa. His statement: (al-su' with damm is the object of la tazunn, and al-zann means accusation.) His statement: (it is a conjunction upon 'an la tazunn': in some copies without the word 'an, which is apparent, and in most copies with the word 'an, which is a slip of the scribe's pen, having no justification except that it is said: the conjunction is upon 'la tazunn' in 'an la tazunn' because it is clear that conjunction upon the entire phrase is not correct, and he chose this expression due to the ugliness of separating the relative from the clause.) His statement: (that the impossible etc. because the mental form exists externally as one of the psychological qualities and is united with the known. If the impossible had a particular form in the mind, its quiddity would exist externally with original existence to have effects upon it, and at least the person would be knowledgeable of it. Then its quiddity would not necessitate the impossibility of existence. This is like the Necessary [Existent] which has no particular form in the mind, otherwise its quiddity would be established by another in the outside. Unlike the possible non-existent, for the obtaining of its particular form in the mind only requires that it have original existence in terms of its subsistence in the intellect, and it has no existence outside the mind, and there is no problem in that. His statement: 'so its conception' i.e. the conception of the impossible etc. in a chapter of the first article of the fifth [book] of The Logic of al-Shifa. And here there is a doubt: How can the non-existent whose essence is impossible of existence be conceived when asked about it as to what it is, so that after that one seeks whether it is [existent] or not? For if no meaning of it obtains in the mind, how can it be conceived as existent or non-existent? And the impossible has no form in existence, so how can a form of it exist in the mind such that the conceived thing is its meaning? We respond to this: This impossible is either simple with no composition... His statement: (the conjunction upon 'an la tazunn': it is not hidden that it is a conjunction upon 'la tazunn', and he only brought the word 'an in the conjoined upon to indicate by the use of the accusative operator that it is accusative.)
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument regarding the possibility of knowledge without a known object (علم لا معلوم له), a thesis attributed to Abu Hashim. The author begins with a preliminary discussion on necessary knowledge (التصديق الضروري), clarifying that 'prior knowledge' (العلم السابق) in the definition of necessary knowledge means prior affirmation (تصديق), not prior conception. This is necessary to avoid the paradox that necessary affirmations would depend on prior necessary concepts, which would make them non-necessary. The author then turns to the main topic: Abu Hashim's claim that there is knowledge of the impossible (المستحيل). Since the impossible is 'not a thing' (ليس بشيء) and the known (المعلوم) is defined as a thing, knowledge of the impossible would be knowledge without a known object. Al-Razi objects that this is contradictory, as 'the known' is precisely that to which knowledge is attached. Al-Amidi suggests a terminological solution: one may simply refuse to call the impossible 'known', but the author deems this useless. The author provides the resolution by invoking Ibn Sina's doctrine that the impossible has no form (صورة) in the mind. That is, we cannot conceive the impossible in its particular essence; rather, we conceive it in a secondary way, e.g., as a composition of incompatible attributes. The long gloss that follows elaborates on this: if the impossible had a specific mental form, its quiddity would have to exist externally, which is false. This is compared to the Necessary Existent (الواجب), which also has no particular form in the mind. The passage ends with a doubt (شك) about how the impossible can be conceived at all, and the beginning of an answer distinguishing simple and composite impossibles. The text is cut off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
علم لا معلوم له
المعلوم
المستحيل
الشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Amidi's terminological solution resolves the problem.
Correction: The author explicitly says the stipulation has no benefit (لا فائدة فيه), because the real issue is whether knowledge can have an object that is not a thing.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's main objection to Abu Hashim's claim?
A) The impossible is actually a thing, so it can be a known object.
B) The phrase 'knowledge without a known object' is contradictory because 'known' means what knowledge is attached to.
C) Knowledge of the impossible is impossible by consensus.
D) The impossible cannot be known at all.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's claim that knowledge of the impossible is knowledge without a known object faces strong objections based on the definition of 'known'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about something impossible—like a belief you know can never be true—do you still find yourself mentally investing in it? Why is it so hard to let go of 'knowledge' that has no real object in your life?
?
Al-Razi shows that if you know something, it is by definition known. Is there a truth about yourself that you know but refuse to acknowledge? What would it cost you to call it by its name?
?
Al-Amidi's semantic fix is dismissed as useless. Are there areas in your faith where you use comfortable labels to avoid facing a deeper unresolved issue? What would honest inquiry demand of you today?

The Resolution: Ibn Sina on Mental Forms
Resolves the problem using Ibn Sina's view on mental forms of the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the problem by adopting Ibn Sina's view that the impossible has no mental form, so its conception is indirect.

Core Idea
The impossible cannot be conceived in its essence, only as a composition of incompatible attributes.

After presenting the objections, the author provides the fair-minded resolution. He advises not to suspect error in Abu Hashim's words but to interpret them in light of Ibn Sina's doctrine: the impossible does not have a form (صورة) in the mind. We cannot conceive it in its particular essence; rather, we conceive it indirectly, e.g., as 'the conjunction of contradictories'. This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's fair-minded principle
The author advises to interpret Abu Hashim's words charitably, seeking a valid interpretation rather than accusing him of error.
This sets the tone for the resolution.
2
Understand Ibn Sina's doctrine
Ibn Sina held that the impossible has no form in the mind; we have no way to perceive it in itself.
This means there is no mental image corresponding to the impossible as such.
3
Grasp the resolution
Thus, 'knowledge of the impossible' does not require a known object in the sense of a real thing. The conception of the impossible is only of its incompatibility, not of a positive essence.
This preserves the principle that all knowledge requires an object, but the object here is mental and indirect.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge of the impossible requires a real external object corresponding to the impossible.
Correction: Ibn Sina explains that the impossible has no mental form; we conceive it only indirectly as a composite of incompatible attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina as cited by the author, why is there no real contradiction in speaking of 'knowledge of the impossible'?
A) Because the impossible is actually possible, so it can be known.
B) Because the impossible has no form in the mind, so its 'knowledge' is indirect and does not require a real object.
C) Because al-Amidi's terminological solution solves the problem.
D) Because the impossible is a thing, so it can be a known object.

Key Takeaway
The resolution lies in recognizing that the impossible has no mental form, so 'knowledge of the impossible' is only an indirect conception of incompatible attributes, not knowledge of a real object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the impossible has no mental form, then every time you think you have grasped something like 'God creating another god' you are only combining incompatible attributes. How does this realization humble your understanding and remind you of the limits of human reason?

The Will is Independent of Belief in Benefit: Three Proofs
The author proves that will is not conditioned by belief in benefit using three empirical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses empirical examples to demonstrate that the will can act without any belief in benefit or inclination.

Core Idea
The will can move without any perceived benefit.

This lesson presents the author's central argument that the will is not dependent on a belief in benefit or an inclination. He clarifies his position by distinguishing between a preponderant and a motive, then gives three compelling examples from ordinary experience that are known by necessity. These examples show that the will can choose between equal options without any motive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's initial clarification: he does not deny a preponderant, but denies a motive from belief.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'preponderant' and 'motive'.
2
Identify the three examples
Find each of the three examples: fleeing person, thirsty person, hungry person. For each, note how the author argues that will operates without any belief in benefit or inclination.
The examples are known by necessity.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
The author concludes that if will exists without belief, then it exists without the inclination that follows belief, since a follower cannot exist without the followed.

على ميل يتبعه، بل يرجح أحدهما ) على الآخر ( بمجرد الإرادة لا أقول : لا يكون لفعل مرجح ) على عدمه فإنَّ الهارب بإرادته مرجح إياه على تركه، (بل) أقول : (لا يكون إليه ) أي إلى الفعل ( داع) باعث للفاعل عليه من اعتقاد النفع أو ميل تابع له، ) ومعلوم بالضرورة أنه من دهشته ) وحيرته ( لا يخطر بباله طلب مرجح يختار بسببه أحدهما، بل لا يطلب ولا يتصور في تلك الحالة سوى النجاة (و) معلوم بالضرورة أيضاً ( أنه لو لم يجد المرجح لم يتوقف متفكراً ) فيه ( حتى يفترسه السبع، وكذلك العطشان إذا كان عنده قد حان من ماء وفرض استواؤهما من جميع الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما بلا داع له يرجحه في اعتقاده ) على الآخر ) وكذلك جائع عنده رغيفان ) متساويان من جميع الجهات فإنه يختار أحدهما من غير داع يدعوه إليه، وإذا ثبت في هذه الأمثلة وجود الإرادة دون اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه ثبت وجودها بدون الميل التابع لهما، إذ لا وجود للتابع بدون المتابوع
Upon an inclination that follows it, but he favors one of them over the other by mere will. I do not say: there is no preponderant for the act over its non-existence, for the fleeing person by his will preponderates it over its abandonment. Rather I say: there is not to it, that is, to the act, a motive (dāʿ) that impels the agent to it from a belief in benefit or an inclination following it. And it is known by necessity that from his bewilderment and confusion he does not conceive in his mind seeking a preponderant by which he would choose one of them; rather he does not seek nor conceive in that state anything but escape. And it is also known by necessity that if he did not find a preponderant he would not stop to reflect until the beast devours him. Likewise, the thirsty person when he has two pitchers of water and the two are assumed equal in all respects, then he chooses one of them without a motive that would give it preponderance in his belief over the other. Likewise, a hungry person with two loaves equal in all respects, then he chooses one of them without a motive calling him to it. And if it is established in these examples the existence of will without belief in benefit or opinion of it, it is established its existence without the inclination that follows them, for there is no existence for the follower without the followed.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the will is not conditioned by a belief in benefit or an inclination. The author first clarifies his position: he does not deny that the act has a preponderant over non-existence (he concedes the fleeing person preponderates the act by his will), but he denies that the act has a motive (dāʿ) from belief or inclination. Then he presents three empirical examples known by necessity: (1) a fleeing person in panic does not seek or conceive any preponderant; he only desires escape. If he found no preponderant he would not pause to reflect. (2) A thirsty person with two equal pitchers of water chooses one without a motive. (3) A hungry person with two equal loaves chooses one without a motive. From these examples, the author concludes that will exists without belief in benefit or opinion, and therefore without the inclination that follows them, since the follower (inclination) cannot exist without the followed (belief).

اعتقاد النفع
ميل
مرجح

ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will always requires a perceived benefit or inclination to act.
Correction: The author shows through three examples that will can function without any belief in benefit or inclination, as in cases of equal options.

Checkpoint

A) A belief in benefit
B) An inclination
C) Neither a belief nor an inclination
D) A preponderator from outside

Key Takeaway
The will can operate without any belief in benefit or inclination, as demonstrated by the three examples of equal choices.

?

?
If your will can operate independently of any perceived benefit, then are you still fully accountable for such choices? How does this lesson change your understanding of responsibility before Allah?

Defending Equality of Alternatives
The author answers the objection that the examples involve a concealed preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of equal alternatives without a known preponderator, addressing the Mu'tazila's objection and considering unconscious influences.

Core Idea
Choice can occur between equal alternatives without a perceived preponderator.

The author has presented examples of equal alternatives (two paths, two pitchers, two loaves). Now an objector argues that actual escape is preponderant over staying, so there is inequality. The author responds by clarifying that equality is between the two specific actions (traversing each path) not between act and omission. He then concedes that the Mu'tazila might invoke an unconscious preponderator due to shock, and also reports a naturalistic explanation based on physical strength.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice the objector's claim that the act (escape) is preponderant over omission (staying), so there is no real equality.
2
Understand the author's response
The author redefines equality: traversing one path implies omitting the other, so each traversal is paired with an omission, making the two act-omission pairs equal.
The equality is between the two alternatives, not between act and omission.
3
Explore the Mu'tazila's counter
The Mu'tazila argue that a belief-based preponderator may have been present but not remembered due to shock (دهشة), so the person is unaware of it.
This shows the Mu'tazila's commitment to a preponderator in all choices.
4
Consider the naturalistic explanation
Some suggest that when paths are equal, nature (طبيعة) causes one to go left because the right side is stronger, pushing the body.
This is a physical, not rational, preponderator.
5
Reflect on the dialectical method
Notice how the author presents multiple perspectives (his own, Mu'tazila, naturalistic) without fully endorsing any, showing the complexity of the issue.
This is typical of kalam: testing arguments from all sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: من البين أن الفعل في هذه الأمثلة راجح على الترك، فلا تساوي فيما بينهما . قلنا : سلوك أحد الطريقين يستلزم ترك سلوك الآخر وبالعكس، فإذا استوى السلوكان فقد استوى سلوك أحدهما وتركه على وجه مخصوص، وهو أن يتركه سالكاً للآخر، وأيضاً السلوكان أمران مقدوران متساويان وقد رجح أحدهما بلا داع إليه وهو المطلوب، نعم للمعتزلة أن يقولوا : ليس يلزم من فرض التساوي وقوعه ولا بد في هذه الصور المفروضة من مرجح بحسب اعتقاده إذ لولاه لم يختر شيئاً مما فرض تساويه وليس يلزم من الشعور بالمرجح الشعور بذلك الشعور فلعل الدهشة المذكورة صارت سبباً لعدم استثبات الشعور في الحافظة، فلأجل ذلك لا يعرف الهارب الآن أنه كان له شعور بالمرجح في تلك الحالة هذا، وقد قيل: إذا فرض تساوي الطريقين في النجاة فإن طبيعته تقتضي سلوك الطريق الذي على يساره لأن القوة في اليمين أكثر، والقوي يدفع الضعيف، كما هو المشاهد فيمن يدور على عقبه، وأما في القدحين والرغيفين فيختار ما هو الأقرب إلى اليمين
For if it is said: It is evident that the act in these examples is preponderant over omission, so there is no equality between them. We said: Traversing one of the two paths necessitates omitting the other and vice versa. So when the two traver sals are equal, then the traversing of one and its omission become equal in a specific manner, namely that he omits it while traversing the other. Also, the two traversals are two equally possible acts, and one of them has been preponderated without a motive to it, and that is what is sought. Yes, the Mu'tazila may say: It does not follow from the assumption of equality that it occurs, and there must be in these supposed cases a preponderant according to his belief, for otherwise he would not choose anything from what was assumed equal. And it does not follow from awareness of the preponderant awareness of that awareness; perhaps the mentioned bewilderment became a cause for the non-fixation of awareness in the memory, and therefore the fleeing person does not now know that he had awareness of the preponderant in that state. This, and it has been said: If the two paths are assumed equal in escape, then his nature dictates traversing the path on his left because the strength in the right is greater, and the strong pushes the weak, as is observed in one who turns on his heel. As for the two pitchers and the two loaves, he chooses what is nearer to the right.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage contains a further objection and response. First, an objector says: In the examples, the act is obviously preponderant over omission (escape is chosen over staying), so there is no real equality. The author responds: Traversing one path entails omitting the other, so the two actions are equal in that one omission is paired with each traversal. The two traversals are equally possible, and one is chosen without a motive—that is the point. Then the author concedes that the Mu'tazila might argue: equality does not guarantee occurrence; a belief-based preponderant might exist unconsciously due to shock, lack of memory, etc. The author reports this view without fully endorsing it. Finally, he mentions a naturalistic explanation: if paths are equal, one tends to go left because of right-side strength dynamics, and for food/drink, one chooses what is nearer to the right. This passage shows the dialectical nature of the argument.

Correction: The author clarifies that equality is between the two alternatives (going left vs. going right), not between the act and its omission. Each traversal is paired with an omission of the other, making the alternatives equal.

The author defends the possibility of choice between genuinely equal alternatives by redefining equality and acknowledging that preponderators may be unconscious or natural rather than rationally known.

?

?
If your body's natural inclinations (like leftward bias) can steer your choices without your conscious consent, how much control do you truly have? Does this humbling thought lead you to depend more on Allah and less on your own perceived autonomy? What one change will you make today to surrender your choices to His will?

Will is Different from Desire: Proof and Refutation
Al-Iji argues that will differs from desire because will is not within our power, and refutes al-Jubba'i's objection of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Iji argues that will is different from desire and how he defends his position against al-Jubba'i's objection of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Will is not under our power, otherwise an infinite chain would result.

This lesson examines a key argument for distinguishing will from desire. Al-Iji argues that our will is not within our power, and he defends this against al-Jubba'i's objection of infinite regress. Understanding this distinction is crucial for the study of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: claim, proof, exception, objection, response.
2
Identify the claim and proof
Identify al-Iji's claim that will differs from desire and his proof based on our will not being within our power.
The proof relies on avoiding infinite regress.
3
Understand the exception
Note the exception: if God gives us power over will, scholars differ on whether that will is willed.
This shows the argument's scope.
4
Examine al-Jubba'i's objection
Al-Jubba'i argues that it is impossible for the agent to will their own will, as that leads to infinite regress.
He uses the same regress reasoning against al-Iji.
5
Learn the response
The rebuttal distinguishes between acquired and necessary will, showing regress can be cut by a divinely created necessary will.
This preserves the original claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد الرابع : الإرادة مغايرة للشهوة بنفسها لأن إرادتنا ليست مقدورة لنا، وإلا احتاج حصولها فينا إلى إرادة أخرى، وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى. اللهم إلا أن يذكروا هذا الفرق على تقدير إقدار الله تعالى إيانا على الإرادة. وقال الجبائي : يستحيل كون الفاعل للإرادة مريداً لها بإرادة أخرى. واحتج بأن الإرادة المقدورة لو كانت مرادة للفاعل لكانت تلك الإرادة الثانية مفتقرة إلى إرادة ثالثة والثالثة إلى رابعة وهلم جراً إلى ما لا نهاية له، ويلزم التسلسل المحال. ورد بأن التسلسل إنما يلزم أن لو كانت كل إرادة مكتسبة مرادة مكتسبة، وليس يلزم ذلك، بل أمكن قطع التسلسل بالانتهاء إلى إرادة ضرورية حاصلة للفاعل يخلق الله تعالى.
Third type - Fourth purpose: Will is different from desire in itself because our will is not within our power; otherwise its occurrence in us would need another will, and so on ad infinitum. Except that they mention this difference on the assumption that God gives us power over the will. Al-Jubba'i said: It is impossible for the agent of the will to will it with another will. He argued that if the will that is within power were willed by the agent, then that second will would need a third will, and the third a fourth, and so on without end, leading to the impossible infinite regress. It was responded that infinite regress would only follow if every acquired will were willed and acquired; that does not necessarily follow, rather it is possible to cut the regress by ending at a necessary will occurring to the agent that God creates.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the difference between will (al-irada) and desire (al-shahwa). The author states that will is different from desire in itself (bi nafsiha) because our will is not within our power (ghayr maqdurat lana). The proof: if our will were within our power, then its occurrence in us would require another will, leading to infinite regress. The author then mentions an exception: if God gives us power over the will, then scholars differ on whether that will is willed. Al-Jubba'i objects that it is impossible for the agent of the will to will it with another will, because that would lead to regress. The response: regress only occurs if every will is acquired and willed, but it can be cut by ending at a necessary will (irada daruriyya) that God creates.

الشهوة
مقدورة
التسلسل
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will is always within our power because we can choose freely.
Correction: The passage argues that our will is not within our power; if it were, infinite regress would result, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is will different from desire?
A) Because will is always within our power
B) Because our will is not within our power
C) Because desire is always willed
D) Because will is never present

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji establishes that will is different from desire because it is not within our power, and defends this view by showing that infinite regress is avoided by a necessary will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe you freely choose your will. But this lesson argues your will is not within your power. Examine a recent decision you made. Were you truly the originator of the will behind it, or was it given to you? How does this reshape your gratitude to Allah for every intention you act upon?
?
Al-Jubba'i thought infinite regress disproved will being willed. But the response shows the regress ends with a necessary will from God. When you consider your own will, do you feel the weight of being dependent on God's creative act for every intention? How does that change how you begin each action, from the simplest to the most significant?
?
You often struggle with desires that contradict your faith. This lesson distinguishes will from desire. What does it mean for your struggle against sin that your will itself is not directly under your power? How does that drive you to rely on Allah for the very will to obey, rather than simply trying harder on your own?

Will vs Wish: Definition and Scholarly Consensus
Al-Iji defines will as distinct from wish, limiting it to possible concurrent things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between will and wish in Islamic theology, and why scholars agree they are different.

Core Idea
Will is for possible present things; wish can reach into the impossible and the past.

In this passage, al-Iji draws a sharp line between will and wish. While these may seem similar, theologians show that will has a restricted scope—only possible concurrent things—whereas wish freely extends to impossibles and past events. This distinction is agreed upon by both Ash'ari and Mu'tazili investigators.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Iji's definition of will
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how al-Iji first states that will is other than wish and then defines its scope: only possible concurrent things.
The phrase 'maqdur muqarin' means something that is possible and exists at the time of the will.
2
Contrast with the scope of wish
Wish is broader: it can attach to the inherently impossible and to past events. Understand that this contrast is the core distinction.
Example of wish for impossible: wishing for a partner for God; wish for past: wishing yesterday had been different.
3
Identify the mistaken view
Some groups thought wish is a type of will, defining it as willing what one knows or doubts will not occur. Note that this is rejected by the investigators.
4
Appreciate the consensus
The passage ends with the agreement of both Ash'ari and Mu'tazili scholars that will and wish are different. Reflect on why this matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد الخامس : الإرادة والتمني عند أهل التحقيق. أنها غير التمني فإنها لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور مقارن لها عند أهل التحقيق، والتمني قد يتعلق بالمحال الذاتي وبالماضي. وقد توهم جماعة أن التمني نوع من الإرادة، حتى عرفوه بأنه إرادة ما علم أنه لا يقع أو شك وقوعه. واتفق المحققون من الأشاعرة والمعتزلة على أن التمني غير الإرادة. والميل الذي يسمونه إرادة هو بالتمني أشبه منه بالإرادة. فتأمل.
Third type - Fifth purpose: Will and wish according to the people of investigation. It (i.e., will) is other than wish, for it does not attach except to a possible concurrent (mumkin muqarin) thing according to the people of investigation, while wish may attach to the inherently impossible (al-muhal al-dhati) and to the past. And a group has imagined that wish is a type of will, so much so that they defined it as willing what is known not to occur or whose occurrence is doubted. And the investigators among the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila have agreed that wish is other than will. And the inclination (al-mayl) which they call will is more similar to wish than to will. So reflect.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between will (al-irada) and wish (al-tamanni). The author states that will does not attach except to a possible concurrent thing (maqdur muqarin), meaning something that is possible and exists at the time of the will or at its time (according to the people of investigation). In contrast, wish can attach to the inherently impossible (like the existence of a partner with God) and to the past (like wishing that something in the past had been different). The author notes that some groups falsely considered wish as a type of will, defining it as willing what one knows or doubts will not occur. However, the investigators from both the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila agree that wish is distinct from will. Finally, the author remarks that the inclination (al-mayl) which is sometimes called will is actually more similar to wish than to true will, because it can attach to non-possible things. The reader is told to reflect (tata'mmal).

Key Terms
التمني
المقدور المقارن
المحال الذاتي
الماضي
الأشاعرة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wish is a type of will because both involve desire.
Correction: Will is only for possible concurrent things, while wish can be for impossibles and the past, so they are distinct.

Checkpoint

A) Will is for possible concurrent things, wish is only for impossibles
B) Will is for possible concurrent things, wish can be for impossibles and past
C) Will is for past, wish is for future
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
Will is restricted to possible concurrent things, while wish extends to the impossible and the past, a distinction agreed upon by Ash'ari and Mu'tazili scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
How often do you confuse wishful thinking with a resolute will? What practical step can you take today to align your will with what is possible and pleasing to Allah, rather than indulging in idle wishes?

Refutation of Identity Between Willing and Hating the Opposite
Al-Jurjani refutes the identity claim using awareness as a separator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why willing a thing cannot be identical to hating its opposite, based on the condition of awareness.

Core Idea
Will and hatred are distinct because will can exist without awareness of the opposite.

Al-Jurjani tackles a subtle claim: that when you will something, you necessarily hate its opposite, and this hatred is the same as your will. He refutes this by pointing to the role of awareness — we often will things without even thinking of their opposites, showing that will and hatred are separate realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: إرادة (will), كراهة (hatred), ضد (opposite), شعور (awareness).
2
Identify the claim being refuted
Find where the opponent's claim is stated: that willing a thing is identical to hating its opposite.
The claim is presented at the beginning: 'إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده'.
3
Notice the key condition: awareness
The refutation hinges on the condition that hating the opposite requires awareness (شعور), but will can occur without it.
The text says: 'شرط كراهة الضد الشعورية اتفاقا وضرورة'.
4
Understand the counterexample
Consider a scenario where something occurs to the mind and one wills it while heedless of its opposite. In that case, will exists without hatred.
This shows that will and hatred separate, proving they are not identical.
5
Conclude that will and hatred are distinct
Since will can exist without hatred, they cannot be the same thing. The passage concludes with the entailment of a thing for itself not depending on a condition.
The final point: 'فلا تكون الإرادة نفس تلك الكراهة'.

Source Text

قوله : (كالنوم إلخ ) وكالشك فإنه عند العدم والظن فاستلزام الشيء لنفسه على تقدير التغاير الاعتباري، مما لا يلتفت إليه لأن الشيء لا يكون عين شيء في حال دون حال، ولأنه لوجود الكراهة حال عدم الشعور .
مع التغاير المصطلح، لأن صحة الانفكاك معتبرة فيه قلت استدلالهم على العينية بانتفاء المغايرة، حيث قالوا : إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده بعينها إذ لو كانت غيرها إلخ ينافي حمل التغاير على المصطلح ، إذ لا يلزم من عدم التغاير الاصطلاحي العينية حينئذ لجواز التلازم . قوله : ( كالنوم هو ضد للعلم والقدرة) كون النوم ضداً للقدرة عند بعض الأشاعرة، وأما عند المعتزلة وكثير من الأشاعرة فهو ضد للعلم لا للقدرة . قوله : ( وهو أن شرط كراهة الضد إلخ وزاد في شرح المقاصد أن شرط إرادة الضد الشعور به أيضاً، فقيل عليه إنه لغو في البين وليس كذلك بل فيه تأكيد الانفكاك فإنه إذا أريد هذا ولم يكره ذلك المجهول أو كره ذلك، ولم يرد هذا المجهول، فقد تأكد معنی التغاير.
قوله: (فاستلزام الشيء لنفسه إلخ إطلاق الاستلزام مبني على اعتبار التغاير، وقد يقال : مراد الشيخ ومتابعيه أن إرادة الشيء كراهة الضد، لكن بتعلق آخر للضد، فالشعور بالضد شرط هذا التعلق فلا يدل على ما ذكره على تغايرهما بالذات وفيه تأمل.

The third type - the sixth purpose: Willing a thing is hatred of its opposite. (Then the will) attached to the thing (separates) at that time (from hatred of the opposite, so it is not) the will itself. With the terminological distinction, because the validity of separation is considered in it. His saying: (like sleep is opposite to knowledge and power) that sleep is opposite to power according to some Ash'aris, but according to the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris it is opposite to knowledge, not power. His saying: (and that the condition of hatred of the opposite etc.) and in Sharh al-Maqasid he added that the condition of willing the opposite is awareness of it also, so it was said against him that it is superfluous, but it is not so; rather, it contains an emphasis on separation, for if this is willed and that unknown is not hated, or that is hated and this unknown is not willed, then the meaning of distinction is confirmed. His saying: (the entailment of a thing for itself etc.) the absolute entailment is based on considering distinction, and it may be said: the shaykh and his followers mean that willing a thing is hatred of the opposite, but with another attachment to the opposite, so awareness of the opposite is a condition of this attachment, so it does not indicate what he mentioned about their being essentially distinct, and it requires consideration. (unclear) not from what His saying: (what is not to be regarded because one's saying: Zayd is identical to 'Amr in some states, and in some states it is heard; in summary, the essence of will does not differ by awareness of the opposite of the intended and lack of awareness of it, so there is no basis for claiming that willing a thing whose opposite is perceived is the very hatred of the perceived opposite, and willing a thing whose opposite is not perceived is other than its hatred, as is not hidden from the fair-minded.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that willing a thing (irada) is identical to hating its opposite (karaha diddih). The argument proceeds as follows: The proponent claims that when one wills something, one necessarily hates its opposite, and this hatred is identical to the will. The refutation is based on the condition of awareness (shu'ur). The critic argues that hatred of the opposite requires awareness of the opposite, but will can occur without such awareness—for example, when something occurs to the mind while the person is heedless of its opposite. In that case, the will exists without hatred, proving they are distinct. Some try to salvage the identity claim by restricting it to cases where awareness is present, but the author dismisses this as untenable because a thing cannot be identical to something else in some states and not others; the essence of will does not change. The passage also includes glosses that discuss examples (sleep as opposite to knowledge/power), the addition in Sharh al-Maqasid that both will and hatred require awareness, and the argument that even if there is no terminological distinction, identity does not follow due to possible concomitance. The overall conclusion is that irada and karaha are distinct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That willing a thing is exactly the same as hating its opposite because they always occur together.
Correction: The passage shows that will can exist without hatred when one is unaware of the opposite, so they are not identical, only often concomitant.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that willing a thing is identical to hating its opposite?
A) Because hatred is always stronger than will.
B) Because will can occur without awareness of the opposite, so they separate.
C) Because the opposite does not exist.
D) Because the shaykh never made such a claim.

Key Takeaway
The identity between willing and hating the opposite is refuted because will can occur without awareness of the opposite, proving they are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you strongly willed something—a goal, a desire—without ever stopping to consider its opposite. What does the fact that your will did not require hatred of the opposite reveal about the nature of your intention? Are you willing to examine your uninformed desires?
?
The author says hatred requires awareness. Is there a person or thing you hate without truly knowing why? What would it take to bring awareness to that hatred, and how might it change you to confront its opposite?
?
If your will can exist apart from hatred, what does that mean for how you judge your own feelings? Are you willing to admit that your will and your aversions may not be as unified as you assume, and that this separation requires honest self-examination?

The Debate: Does Willing Entail Hatred of the Opposite?
Al-Qadi and al-Ghazali argue for entailment; the author rejects it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central question and the two opposing positions of al-Qadi and al-Ghazali versus the author.

Core Idea
Willing something does not necessarily require hating its opposite—it may be possible to will both.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif sets up a classic kalam dispute: does willing something, while aware of its opposite, necessarily entail that the opposite is hated? Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and al-Ghazali argue for entailment; the author of the commentary takes the contrary view. Let's begin by understanding the question and the two positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening question
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the setup: the issue is whether willing a thing (irada) entails hatred (karaha) of its opposite under the condition of awareness (shu'ur).
Notice that the author immediately clarifies it's not absolute entailment.
2
Identify the two positions
Al-Qadi and al-Ghazali affirm the entailment; the author holds the contrary view. Find the phrases marking these positions.
The phrase 'wa al-dhahir 'inda al-musannif khilafuh' signals the author's view.
3
Note the qualification: awareness
The debate is specifically under awareness of the opposite. Without awareness, entailment is not claimed.
This limits the scope of the disagreement.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: if willing necessarily entailed hating the opposite, certain human desires (like wanting two beneficial things) would be contradictory. The author's view allows more flexibility.

) فهل الإرادة مستلزمة لكراهة الضد ) ؛ لا مطلقاً إذ قد تبين انفكاكها عن الكراهة في بعض الصور، بل (بشرط الشعور به ( أي بالضد ( مختلف فيه قال القاضي ) أبو بكر (و) الإمام ( الغزالي : مستلزمة ) أي إرادة الشيء مع الشعور بضده، تستلزم كون الضد مكروهاً . عند ذلك المريد ( والظاهر) عند المصنف ) خلافه لجواز أن يريد ( الشخص بحسب ( الضدين كل واحد منهما ( من وجه إرادة على السوية، أو يترجح أحدهما ما فيه من نفع راجح على نفع الآخر، فيكونان مرادين لا على السوية، وهذا الظاهر
قوله : ( تستلزم كون الضد مكروهاً)، إذ لو لم يكن مكروهاً لجاز أن يكون مراداً، فيلزم جواز إرادة الضدين فاندفع ما قيل إنه يجوز أن لا تتعلق بالضد كراهة ولا إرادة الكثير من المشعور بها .
قوله : ( لجواز إلخ) في شرح المقاصد هذا لا يبطل حكم القاضي بالاستلزام، ولا يثبت خلافه، لأنه إذا جاز إرادة الضدين من وجه يجوز كراهة كل منهما من وجه، نعم إنه يصلح في معرض الجواب عن استدلالهم المذكور بمنع استحالة جواز إرادة الضدين لجواز أن يريد الشخص الضدين إلخ حتى لو أجيب بما أجاب به الشارح من أن متعلق الإرادة لا بد أن يكون مقارناً لها، فيلزم من إرادة الضدين اجتماعهما كان كلاماً على السند، انتهى . أقول : المراد أنه أن يريد الشخص الضدين من وجه من غير كراهة شيء منها بأن يكون وقوع كل واحد منهما منفعة مع عدم المضرة، فحينئذ يتحقق إرادة الضدين من غير كراهتهما بوجه فبطل الحكم بالاستلزام .
قوله: (وتستلزم كون الضد مكروهاً ) قال في شرح المقاصد : لو صح هذا لكان كراهة الشيء مستلزمة لإرادة ضده المشعور به فيلزم من إرادة الشيء الذي له ضدان أن يكون كل منهما مكروهاً لكونه ضد المراد ومراداً لكونه ضد المكروه ولا محيص إلا بتجويزه عند تغاير الجهتين، أو تخصيص الدعوى بماله ضد واحد إلى هاهنا كلامه وجوابه الملازمة المذكورة، فإن دليل استلزام إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده لزوم إرادة الضدين على تقدير عدم الاستلزام، كما سيذكره الآن، ومثل هذا الدليل ليس بقائم على أن كراهة الشيء مستلزمة لإرادة ضده لجواز كراهة الضدين بخلاف إرادتهما عند الأشاعرة . قوله : ( لجواز أن يريد (الضدين وأيضاً يجوز أن لا يتعلق بالضد إرادة ولا كراهة ككثير من الأمور المشعور بها، واعلم أن ما ذكره المصنف من جواز إرادة الضدين لا يصح في معرض إبطال حكم القاضي والغزالي بالاستلزام المذكور لجواز أن يكون كل منهما مكروهاً أيضاً بجهة وإنما يصح في معرض الجواب عما لهما من الدليل الذي لم يذكره المصنف، وهو أنه لو لم يكن ضد المراد المشعور به مكروهاً لكان مراداً فيلزم إرادة الضدين وهو محال، لأن الإرادتين المتعلقتين بالضدين متضادتان فتأمل.
The third type - the sixth purpose: Willing a thing is hatred of its opposite. (So is the will necessarily consequent upon hatred of the opposite?) Not absolutely, for its separation from hatred in some cases has become evident, but (with the condition of awareness of it) i.e. of the opposite (it is disputed. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and Imam al-Ghazali said: it is necessarily consequent,) i.e. willing a thing along with awareness of its opposite necessitates that the opposite be hated by that wanter. (And the apparent view) according to the author (is the contrary, because it is possible for a person) in accordance with (both opposites, each one of them) from one aspect, to will equally, or one is preferred due to a more preponderant benefit over the benefit of the other, so they become willed not equally, and this is the apparent view. His saying: (it necessitates that the opposite be hated) for if it were not hated, it would be possible for it to be willed, so the possibility of willing both opposites would be necessary, and thus what was said—that it is possible for neither hatred nor will to attach to the opposite for many perceived things—is repelled. His saying: (because it is possible etc.) In Sharh al-Maqasid: This does not invalidate the judgment of al-Qadi of entailment, nor does it establish its contrary, because if it is possible to will both opposites from one aspect, it is possible to hate each of them from one aspect. Rather, it is suitable in the context of a response to their mentioned argument by denying the impossibility of willing both opposites, because it is possible for a person to will both opposites etc., even if one answers with what the commentator answered—that the object of will must be concurrent with it, so willing both opposites necessitates their simultaneity—it would be speech on the chain. End. I say: the meaning is that it is possible for a person to will both opposites from one aspect without hatred of anything of them, such that the occurrence of each one is beneficial with no harm, then willing both opposites is realized without any hatred of them, so the judgment of entailment is invalidated. His saying: (and it necessitates that the opposite be hated). He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: If this were correct, then hatred of a thing would necessitate willing its perceived opposite, so from willing a thing that has two opposites, each of them would be hated for being opposite of the willed, and willed for being opposite of the hated, and there is no escape except by allowing it when the aspects differ, or specifying the claim to what has one opposite. Up to here is his speech and his answer to the mentioned entailment, for the proof of the entailment of willing a thing for hating its opposite is the necessity of willing both opposites on the assumption of non-entailment, as he will mention now. Such a proof is not established to show that hatred of a thing necessitates willing its opposite, because it is possible to hate both opposites, unlike willing them according to the Ash'aris. His saying: (because it is possible to will the two opposites). Also, it is possible that no will or hatred attaches to the opposite, like many perceived things. And know that what the author mentioned about the possibility of willing both opposites is not valid in the context of refuting the judgment of al-Qadi and al-Ghazali of the mentioned entailment, because it is possible that each of them is also hated from some aspect. Rather, it is valid in the context of responding to their proof which the author did not mention, namely that if the opposite of the perceived willed were not hated, it would be willed, so willing both opposites would be necessary, which is impossible, because the two wills pertaining to the two opposites are mutually contradictory. So reflect.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage addresses the question: When a person is aware of the opposite, does willing a thing necessarily entail that the opposite is hated? Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and al-Ghazali affirm this entailment: if one wills a thing while aware of its opposite, that opposite must be hated. The author (the commentator on al-Mawaqif) holds the apparent contrary view: it is possible for a person to will both opposites from different aspects, either equally or with one preferred due to a greater benefit, without hating either. The glosses discuss the arguments: the proponents argue that if the opposite were not hated, it could be willed, leading to the impossibility of willing two opposites simultaneously. The author's response is that willing both opposites from different aspects is possible, so no impossibility. Furthermore, it is even possible to have neither will nor hatred towards many perceived things. The commentary also notes that the author's argument does not directly refute the entailment claim (since each could be hated from some aspect) but rather responds to the proponents' proof that non-hatred would lead to willing both opposites. The passage ends with a warning to reflect.
