Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Willing something while aware of its opposite always means you hate that opposite.
Correction: The author argues it is possible to will both opposites from different aspects without hatred.

Checkpoint
What is the author's initial position on whether willing a thing entails hating its opposite under awareness?
A) It necessarily entails it.
B) It does not necessarily entail it; one can will both opposites from different aspects.
C) It entails it only if the opposite is unknown.
D) It entails the opposite of hatred.

Key Takeaway
The debate centers on whether awareness of an opposite necessitates hatred; the author favors that it does not, as one can will both opposites from different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you have conflicting desires—e.g., you want to please your parents and also want to follow your own path. According to this lesson, can you genuinely will both without necessarily hating the other? How does that insight challenge your usual approach to inner conflict? Will you now allow yourself to hold both wills without guilt?
?
When you face a sin you are tempted by, do you automatically assume that your desire for the sin means you hate the opposite (obedience)? This lesson suggests that one can desire both from different aspects. Could your struggle be more nuanced? How will you rethink your spiritual battles considering that you might not truly hate obedience, but desire sin from a different angle? Will that understanding change how you seek forgiveness?
?
Consider a relationship where you feel both love and frustration. Does loving someone entail hating their faults? The author says no—you can will both the person and the change in them from different aspects. How can this help you love more purely without resentment? Will you intentionally separate these wills in your heart?

Arguments and Counterarguments on Entailment
The proof for entailment and the author's rebuttal using the possibility of willing both opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof offered for entailment and the author's rebuttal based on the possibility of willing both opposites.

Core Idea
The proof from impossibility of willing both opposites is refuted by the possibility of willing them from different aspects.

Having set up the two positions, the passage now presents the reasoning behind al-Qadi and al-Ghazali's claim: if the opposite were not hated, it could be willed, leading to the impossibility of willing two opposites. The author responds by showing that willing both opposites from different aspects is possible, and even that many perceived things attract neither will nor hatred. Let's examine these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the pro-entaliment argument
The proponents argue: if the opposite were not hated, it could be willed, leading to willing both opposites, which is impossible. Therefore, the opposite must be hated.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Understand the author's response
The author counters by asserting that it is possible to will both opposites from different aspects (e.g., equal benefits or one preferred), so the impossibility claim fails.
The author also notes that many perceived things have neither will nor hatred attached.
3
Consider the commentary's clarification
The commentary in Sharh al-Maqasid notes that the author's response does not directly refute the entailment claim, because each opposite could still be hated from some aspect. But it does refute the proof based on impossibility.
The commentary suggests the author's argument is more effective against the proof than against the claim itself.
4
Reflect on the final warning
The passage ends with a call to reflect (tammal), indicating that the argument has nuances and requires careful thought.

النوع الثالث - المقصد السادس : إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده
) فهل الإرادة مستلزمة لكراهة الضد ) ؛ لا مطلقاً إذ قد تبين انفكاكها عن الكراهة في بعض الصور، بل (بشرط الشعور به ( أي بالضد ( مختلف فيه قال القاضي ) أبو بكر (و) الإمام ( الغزالي : مستلزمة ) أي إرادة الشيء مع الشعور بضده، تستلزم كون الضد مكروهاً . عند ذلك المريد ( والظاهر) عند المصنف ) خلافه لجواز أن يريد ( الشخص بحسب ( الضدين كل واحد منهما ( من وجه إرادة على السوية، أو يترجح أحدهما ما فيه من نفع راجح على نفع الآخر، فيكونان مرادين لا على السوية، وهذا الظاهر
قوله : ( تستلزم كون الضد مكروهاً)، إذ لو لم يكن مكروهاً لجاز أن يكون مراداً، فيلزم جواز إرادة الضدين فاندفع ما قيل إنه يجوز أن لا تتعلق بالضد كراهة ولا إرادة الكثير من المشعور بها .
قوله : ( لجواز إلخ) في شرح المقاصد هذا لا يبطل حكم القاضي بالاستلزام، ولا يثبت خلافه، لأنه إذا جاز إرادة الضدين من وجه يجوز كراهة كل منهما من وجه، نعم إنه يصلح في معرض الجواب عن استدلالهم المذكور بمنع استحالة جواز إرادة الضدين لجواز أن يريد الشخص الضدين إلخ حتى لو أجيب بما أجاب به الشارح من أن متعلق الإرادة لا بد أن يكون مقارناً لها، فيلزم من إرادة الضدين اجتماعهما كان كلاماً على السند، انتهى . أقول : المراد أنه أن يريد الشخص الضدين من وجه من غير كراهة شيء منها بأن يكون وقوع كل واحد منهما منفعة مع عدم المضرة، فحينئذ يتحقق إرادة الضدين من غير كراهتهما بوجه فبطل الحكم بالاستلزام .
قوله: (وتستلزم كون الضد مكروهاً ) قال في شرح المقاصد : لو صح هذا لكان كراهة الشيء مستلزمة لإرادة ضده المشعور به فيلزم من إرادة الشيء الذي له ضدان أن يكون كل منهما مكروهاً لكونه ضد المراد ومراداً لكونه ضد المكروه ولا محيص إلا بتجويزه عند تغاير الجهتين، أو تخصيص الدعوى بماله ضد واحد إلى هاهنا كلامه وجوابه الملازمة المذكورة، فإن دليل استلزام إرادة الشيء كراهة ضده لزوم إرادة الضدين على تقدير عدم الاستلزام، كما سيذكره الآن، ومثل هذا الدليل ليس بقائم على أن كراهة الشيء مستلزمة لإرادة ضده لجواز كراهة الضدين بخلاف إرادتهما عند الأشاعرة . قوله : ( لجواز أن يريد (الضدين وأيضاً يجوز أن لا يتعلق بالضد إرادة ولا كراهة ككثير من الأمور المشعور بها، واعلم أن ما ذكره المصنف من جواز إرادة الضدين لا يصح في معرض إبطال حكم القاضي والغزالي بالاستلزام المذكور لجواز أن يكون كل منهما مكروهاً أيضاً بجهة وإنما يصح في معرض الجواب عما لهما من الدليل الذي لم يذكره المصنف، وهو أنه لو لم يكن ضد المراد المشعور به مكروهاً لكان مراداً فيلزم إرادة الضدين وهو محال، لأن الإرادتين المتعلقتين بالضدين متضادتان فتأمل.
The third type - the sixth purpose: Willing a thing is hatred of its opposite. (So is the will necessarily consequent upon hatred of the opposite?) Not absolutely, for its separation from hatred in some cases has become evident, but (with the condition of awareness of it) i.e. of the opposite (it is disputed. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and Imam al-Ghazali said: it is necessarily consequent,) i.e. willing a thing along with awareness of its opposite necessitates that the opposite be hated by that wanter. (And the apparent view) according to the author (is the contrary, because it is possible for a person) in accordance with (both opposites, each one of them) from one aspect, to will equally, or one is preferred due to a more preponderant benefit over the benefit of the other, so they become willed not equally, and this is the apparent view. His saying: (it necessitates that the opposite be hated) for if it were not hated, it would be possible for it to be willed, so the possibility of willing both opposites would be necessary, and thus what was said—that it is possible for neither hatred nor will to attach to the opposite for many perceived things—is repelled. His saying: (because it is possible etc.) In Sharh al-Maqasid: This does not invalidate the judgment of al-Qadi of entailment, nor does it establish its contrary, because if it is possible to will both opposites from one aspect, it is possible to hate each of them from one aspect. Rather, it is suitable in the context of a response to their mentioned argument by denying the impossibility of willing both opposites, because it is possible for a person to will both opposites etc., even if one answers with what the commentator answered—that the object of will must be concurrent with it, so willing both opposites necessitates their simultaneity—it would be speech on the chain. End. I say: the meaning is that it is possible for a person to will both opposites from one aspect without hatred of anything of them, such that the occurrence of each one is beneficial with no harm, then willing both opposites is realized without any hatred of them, so the judgment of entailment is invalidated. His saying: (and it necessitates that the opposite be hated). He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: If this were correct, then hatred of a thing would necessitate willing its perceived opposite, so from willing a thing that has two opposites, each of them would be hated for being opposite of the willed, and willed for being opposite of the hated, and there is no escape except by allowing it when the aspects differ, or specifying the claim to what has one opposite. Up to here is his speech and his answer to the mentioned entailment, for the proof of the entailment of willing a thing for hating its opposite is the necessity of willing both opposites on the assumption of non-entailment, as he will mention now. Such a proof is not established to show that hatred of a thing necessitates willing its opposite, because it is possible to hate both opposites, unlike willing them according to the Ash'aris. His saying: (because it is possible to will the two opposites). Also, it is possible that no will or hatred attaches to the opposite, like many perceived things. And know that what the author mentioned about the possibility of willing both opposites is not valid in the context of refuting the judgment of al-Qadi and al-Ghazali of the mentioned entailment, because it is possible that each of them is also hated from some aspect. Rather, it is valid in the context of responding to their proof which the author did not mention, namely that if the opposite of the perceived willed were not hated, it would be willed, so willing both opposites would be necessary, which is impossible, because the two wills pertaining to the two opposites are mutually contradictory. So reflect.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage addresses the question: When a person is aware of the opposite, does willing a thing necessarily entail that the opposite is hated? Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and al-Ghazali affirm this entailment: if one wills a thing while aware of its opposite, that opposite must be hated. The author (the commentator on al-Mawaqif) holds the apparent contrary view: it is possible for a person to will both opposites from different aspects, either equally or with one preferred due to a greater benefit, without hating either. The glosses discuss the arguments: the proponents argue that if the opposite were not hated, it could be willed, leading to the impossibility of willing two opposites simultaneously. The author's response is that willing both opposites from different aspects is possible, so no impossibility. Furthermore, it is even possible to have neither will nor hatred towards many perceived things. The commentary also notes that the author's argument does not directly refute the entailment claim (since each could be hated from some aspect) but rather responds to the proponents' proof that non-hatred would lead to willing both opposites. The passage ends with a warning to reflect.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة
ضد
شعور
استلزام
تضاد

Checkpoint
What is the main proof for the entailment according to al-Qadi and al-Ghazali?
A) If the opposite were not hated, it would be willed, leading to willing both opposites, which is impossible.
B) Hatred of the opposite is always felt when willing anything.
C) The opposite is always harmful.
D) The will is never without hatred.

Key Takeaway
The entailment is not necessary because one can will both opposites from different aspects without hating either, and the proof from impossibility is refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself desiring something that conflicts with your values, do you automatically assume you must hate those values? This lesson teaches that desire and aversion can exist from different aspects. In your own life, where have you experienced conflicting desires without necessarily hating either? How does that awareness change how you judge your sincerity?
?
The passage ends with 'so reflect'. Reflect now: Is there a desire you hold that you have assumed must be accompanied by hatred of its opposite? What would it mean for you to honestly acknowledge that you might will both from different aspects, and how does that affect your spiritual accountability?
?
After studying this lesson, what is one concrete step you will take to be more honest with yourself about the complexity of your intentions and aversions, rather than simplifying them into binary opposites?

The Objection to Created Power and the Response from Sharh al-Maqasid
Al-Ījī objects to created power; al-Jurjānī answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari objection to created power and the response that interprets power as disposition to effect rather than actual effect.

Core Idea
Created power is defined by disposition to effect, not actual effect.

In this passage, al-Ījī raises a challenge: the Ash'ari concept of created power (qudra ḥāditha) seems to fit neither of the two standard definitions of power—it is not an actual effect nor a principle of effect. Al-Jurjānī responds by reinterpreting the definition: power means 'disposed to effect,' a subtle but crucial shift. This lesson unpacks the objection and the defense, clarifying how Ash'aris maintain both divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the objection: created power does not satisfy either definition of power (actual effect or principle of effect) because it does not effect anything according to Ash'aris.
Notice the proof given: if an act were by created power, it would conflict with divine power.
2
Examine the response from Sharh al-Maqasid
The answer redefines power as 'disposition to effect' (sha'n al-ta'thir). Created power has this disposition, but because the act occurs by divine power, it does not actually effect the act.
This preserves the definition while maintaining divine causality.
3
Understand the supporting argument
The response relies on the concept of conceptual commonality (ishtirak ma'nawi) between eternal and created power: both are power, but effect is not by essence but by being power.
This refutes the objection that if eternal power is effective, created power would also be effective.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this solution allows Ash'aris to affirm human responsibility (kasb) without compromising divine omnipotence.
This is a key move in Islamic theology regarding free will and determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد عليهما أي على التفسيرين القدرة الحادثة على رأينا معاشر الأشاعرة. فإنها لا تؤثر في فعل أصلاً، فلا تدخل في التفسير الأول، وليست مبدأ الأثر قطعاً، فلا تدخل في التفسير الثاني، وإن كان لها عندنا تعلق بالفعل يسمى ذلك التعلق كسباً. والدليل على أن القدرة الحادثة ليست مؤثرة أنه لو كان فعل العبد بقدرته وتأثيرها فيه وأنه واقع بقدرة الله تعالى أي قدرته تعالى متعلقة بفعل العبد بل هو شك في. أجاب عنه في شرح المقاصد بأنَّ المراد من شأنها التأثير، ولا أن القدرة الحادثة كذلك، لكن لوقوع المقدور بالقدرة لم يؤثر بالفعل ويؤيد ذلك قولنا بحدوث متعلقات القدرة القديمة، وقول المعتزلة بتقدم القدرة الحادثة على الفعل بالزمان هذا، لكن إثبات القدرة الحادثة من شأنها التأثير دونه خرط القتاد كيف وقد قام البرهان على امتناع تأثيرها، والقول بأنَّ القدرة القائمة مؤثرة، فيكون من شأن الحادثة التأثير أيضاً لاشتراكهما في مطلق القدرة، إنما يتم على القول بالاشتراك المعنوي، وبأن تأثير القدرة ليس بخصوصية ذاتها، بل لكونها قدرة .
And there is an objection against them, i.e., against the two interpretations, regarding the created power according to our view, we Ash'aris. For it does not effect an act at all, so it does not enter under the first interpretation, and it is certainly not a principle of effect, so it does not enter under the second interpretation. Although we hold that it has a relation to the act called kasb (acquisition). The proof that created power is not effective is that if the act of the servant were by his power and its effect in it, and that it occurs by the power of God, i.e., His power is related to the act of the servant, but it is a doubt. He (the commentator) answered it in Sharh al-Maqasid that what is meant by power is that it is disposed (sha'n) to effect, not that created power is actually effective. However, because the effect occurs by [divine] power, it does not effect the act. That is supported by our statement that the objects of eternal power occur, and the Mu'tazilite statement that created power precedes the act in time. But proving that created power is disposed to effect is extremely difficult, especially since proof has been established for the impossibility of its effect. The claim that eternal power is effective, so created power would also be disposed to effect because they share the essence of power, is only valid on the view of conceptual commonality (ishtirak ma'nawi), and that the effect of power is not by the specificity of its essence but by its being power.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of power (qudra). The objection states that the created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha), according to the Ash'aris, does not satisfy either of the two interpretations of power. The first interpretation requires power to be effective (muʾaththir), but created power does not effect the act. The second interpretation requires power to be a principle of effect (mabdaʾ al-athar), but created power is not that either. The Ash'aris call its relation to the act 'kasb' (acquisition). The proof is that if the servant's act were by his power, it would conflict with God's power. The answer from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies that the definition of power refers to its disposition (sha'n) to effect, not actual effect. Created power has that disposition, but due to divine power bringing about the act, it does not actually effect it. The response further notes that the idea that eternal power is effective implies created power's disposition to effect is only valid on the view of conceptual commonality (ishtirak ma'nawi) and that effect is not by essence but by being power.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
التفسير الأول
التفسير الثاني
الكسب
شأنها التأثير
الاشتراك المعنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes that for something to be called 'power,' it must actually produce an effect or be a principle of effect.
Correction: The response clarifies that 'power' is defined as disposition to effect, not actual effect. Created power has this disposition even though it does not actually effect due to divine power.

Checkpoint
According to the response in Sharh al-Maqasid, why does created power not actually effect an act?
A) Because it lacks the essence of power
B) Because the act occurs by divine power
C) Because it is not disposed to effect
D) Because it is created and not eternal

Key Takeaway
Created power is defined by its disposition to effect, not by actual effect, reconciling divine omnipotence with human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In moments of success, do you truly believe your efforts are only a disposition and that Allah is the sole actualizer? How does this lesson challenge the pride you feel in your achievements?
?
If your power is only a disposition and not effective, why do you still feel anxiety about your failures? What does that anxiety reveal about your assumption that your power actually causes outcomes?
?
The passage says created power and divine power share the essence of 'power' but differ in actual effect. How does this shape your view of your own limited power in relation to Allah's limitless power? Are you content with being merely disposed while Allah acts?

Refutation of Jahm's Denial of Human Power (Qudra)
Al-Jurjani refutes Jahm's denial of human power and argues the dispute is verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes Jahm's extreme predestinarian view by appealing to intuitive knowledge and clarifying the verbal nature of the dispute.

Core Idea
The dispute over human power is verbal, not substantive; our intuition confirms the attribute even if not effective.

Al-Jurjani tackles the extreme view of Jahm ibn Safwan, who denied that humans have any power at all. He argues that this position contradicts what we know intuitively: we clearly distinguish between a voluntary action and an involuntary one. This lesson unpacks his refutation and shows that the real disagreement is about what to call the attribute, not whether it exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the use of intuitive evidence from everyday experience.
2
Identify the intuitive evidence
Focus on the example of ascending by choice versus falling involuntarily. Ask yourself why this difference is considered obvious and how it challenges Jahm's denial of power.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Follow the response to the objection
Jahm might object that power must be effective. See how Al-Jurjani resolves the problem of mutual opposition between divine and human power by stating that human power is not effective.
4
Understand the verbal dispute
Finally, note how the disagreement reduces to a verbal one: both sides acknowledge the existence of the attribute; the issue is only whether to call it 'power'.
This is a key insight for resolving theological disagreements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنَّ ذلك الفعل مقدور لله تعالى، فإذا فرض أنّ الله أراد شيئاً وأراد العبد ضده إلى آخره ) وأنه ( أي ما ذهب إليه جهم بن صفوان الترمذي من نفي قدرة العبد بالكلية ( غلو) وتجاوز عن الحد ) في الجبر لا توسط بين الجبر والتفويض كما هو الحق ( وأنه ) أي ما ذهب إليه ( مكابرة ) أيضاً ودفع لما هو معلوم بالبديهة ( لأن الفرق بين الصاعد ) إلى موضع عال ( بالاختيار و ( بين (الساقط عن علو ضروري فالأول له اختيار أي له صفة يوجد الصعود عقيبها ويتوهم كونها مؤثرة فيه وتسمى تلك الصفة قدرة واختياراً ( دون الثاني ) إذ ليس له تلك الصفة بالقياس إلى سقوطه ( ويندفع الإشكال ) اللازم من تمانع قدرة الله وقدرة العقد ( بما ذكرناه من عدم تأثير قدرته ) أي قدرة العبد، فلا حاجة في دفعه إلى ما ارتكبه من الغلو ( فإن قال : ( جهم ( لا نريد بالقدرة إلا الصفة المؤثرة وإذ لا تأثير) كما اعترفتم به ( فلا قدرة أيضاً ) كان منازعاً في التسمية ) فإنا نثبت للعبد ذات الصفة المعلومة بالبديهة ونسميها قدرة، فإذا اعترف جهم بتلك الصفة وقال : إنها ليست قدرة لعدم تأثيرها كان نزاعه معنا في إطلاق لفظ القدرة على تلك الصفة، وهو بحث لفظي وإن قال : حقيقة القدرة وماهيتها أنها صفة مؤثرة منعناه بأنَّ التأثير من توابع القدرة، وقد ينفك عنها كما في القدرة الحادثة عندنا.
That act is within the power of Allah the Exalted. Then if it is assumed that Allah willed something and the servant willed its opposite, etc. And that (i.e., what Jahm ibn Safwan al-Tirmidhi went to, of denying the servant's power entirely) is extremism and exceeding the limit in predestination (al-jabr). There is no middle between predestination and delegation (al-tafwīḍ) as is the truth. And that (i.e., what he went to) is also stubbornness and a denial of what is known by intuition (al-badīha), because the difference between one ascending to a high place by choice and one falling from a height necessarily: the first has choice, i.e., he has an attribute by which the ascent occurs subsequent to it, and it is imagined to be effective in it, and that attribute is called power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyār); unlike the second, for he does not have that attribute with respect to his fall. And the problem arising from the mutual opposition of Allah's power and the servant's power is repelled by what we mentioned of the non-effectiveness of his power (i.e., the servant's power); so there is no need in repelling it to resort to the extremism he committed. And if Jahm says, 'We mean by power only the effective attribute, and since there is no effect (as you admit), then there is no power either,' he is disputing over naming. For we affirm for the servant the essence of the attribute known by intuition and we call it power. So if Jahm acknowledges that attribute and says it is not power because of its lack of effect, his dispute with us is over the application of the word 'power' to that attribute, which is a verbal discussion. And if he says that the reality and quiddity of power is that it is an effective attribute, we deny that, because effect (al-ta'thīr) is among the concomitants of power, and it may be separated from it, as in the case of originated power according to us.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents Al-Jurjani's refutation of Jahm ibn Safwan's extreme predestinarian view that the servant has absolutely no power. The author first states that the act in question is within Allah's power, and if we assume conflicting wills, the problem arises. He then characterizes Jahm's position as extremism and stubbornness, because it contradicts intuitive knowledge: we clearly perceive a difference between a voluntary ascent and an involuntary fall. The former involves an attribute that accompanies the action and is imagined to be effective—this is called power and choice. The latter lacks such an attribute. The argument then addresses the possible objection that if the servant's power is not effective, the problem of mutual opposition between divine and human power remains. Al-Jurjani replies that this problem is resolved precisely by the fact that the servant's power is not effective; there is no actual conflict. Finally, he considers Jahm's response that power is defined as an effective attribute, so if the servant's attribute is not effective, it should not be called power. The author counters that this is a mere verbal dispute: we both acknowledge the existence of the attribute known by intuition; the disagreement is only about whether to call it 'power.' He adds that effectiveness is a concomitant of power and may be absent, as in the case of originated power (according to Ash'ari doctrine). The overall argument is that Jahm's denial goes against evident intuition and that the core issue is semantic, not substantive.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
جبر (jabr)
تفويض (tafwīḍ)
بديهة (badīha)
تأثير (ta'thīr)
اختيار (ikhtiyār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jahm believes that since human power is not effective, it should not be called power at all.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explains that the attribute known by intuition is real and is called power; effectiveness is a separate concomitant that may be absent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of the dispute between Jahm and the Ash'aris regarding human power?
A) A substantive disagreement about the existence of the attribute
B) A verbal dispute about the application of the term 'power'
C) A disagreement about the extent of divine power
D) A difference in methodology

Key Takeaway
The human power (qudra) affirmed by the Ash'aris is a real attribute known by intuition, even if it is not effective; the dispute with Jahm is merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act—pray, give charity, or even sin—do you truly believe your choice is real, or do you secretly think it's all Allah's doing with no role for you? How does Al-Jurjani's argument about the intuitive difference between ascending by choice and falling involuntarily confront your own experience? Will you stop dismissing your choices as illusions?
?
The passage says that Jahm's denial is 'stubbornness against the self-evident.' Is there any area of your life where you ignore what is obvious to your conscience because it conflicts with a theological position you hold? What would it cost you to acknowledge your own power and responsibility?
?
Al-Jurjani calls the dispute 'verbal.' Is your faith a matter of words or reality? When you say 'I am powerless,' do you use it to escape accountability? This lesson affirms an attribute of power in you—even if not effective. How will you live differently knowing that you have a real attribute that distinguishes your voluntary actions from involuntary ones?

Ash'ari Proof of Power through Inner Experience
Ash'ari proof of human power by inner experience and rebuttal of Imam's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof of human power through inner experience and how they defend it against the objection of necessity versus choice.

Core Idea
Inner experience shows we have power – the difference between voluntary and involuntary acts is undeniable.

This passage presents the core Ash'ari argument for the existence of human power (al-qudra al-haditha). Rather than relying on scripture or abstract syllogism, the Ash'aris point to an immediate, undeniable experience: the clear difference we perceive between actions we choose and those that happen to us (like trembling). This lesson unpacks that proof and shows how Ash'ari theologians responded to a powerful objection from Imam al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a claim about inner knowledge, then two examples, then an objection and response.
The Arabic is dense; don't worry about every word on first reading.
2
Identify the proof from inner experience
Find the two examples used to demonstrate the necessary distinction between voluntary and involuntary acts. Ask yourself: why does this distinction prove power?
3
Understand the objection and the Ash'ari answer
Read the objection from Imam al-Razi and the two-part response. The key is that impossibility is conditional, not absolute.
The answer relies on the idea that necessity does not negate the equality of possibilities with respect to power itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : إثبات القدرة الحادثة وتعريفها اختلف في طريق إثباتها والحق ما عليه الأشاعرة وهو أنها تعرف ويعلم وجودها بالوجدان حيث قلنا: إن الفرق بين الصاعد بالاختيار والساقط عن علو ضروري، فإنا نجد حالة الصعود أمراً ثابتاً لها دون حالة السقوط، وكذا نجد تفرقة ضرورية بين حركة الارتعاش وحركة الاختيار. وأما اعتراض الإمام بأن الاختيار قبل الفعل باطل عندكم، ومعه ممنوع لامتناع العدم حال الوجود، وحصول الحركة حال ما خلقها الله ضروري وقبله محال فأين الاختيار؟ وأيضاً حصول الفعل عند استواء الدواعي محال، وعند عدم الاستواء يجب الراجح ويمتنع المرجوح فلا يثبت المكنة، فجوابه النقض بأن هذه السلوك مصادمة للبديهة وكل ما هو مصادم للبديهة فهو باطل، والحل بأنا لا نسلم امتناع العدم حال الوجود لجواز أن يقع العدم بدله بل بشرط الوجود، وكذا الحال في الحركة وحصول الفعل عند استواء الدواعي فإن الضروري بشرط خلقها لا في زمان خلقها. والمحال بشرط عند استواء الدواعي لا عند استوائها. وبأن التفرقة ضرورية بالنظر إلى نفس الحركتين فإن حركة البطش بالنظر إلى ذاتها مع قطع النظر عن الأمور الخارجية اختيارية بخلاف حركة الارتعاش. وقد قال: وكذا تجد تفرقة ضرورية بين حركة الارتعاش وحركة الاختيار. وأجيب بأن الضروري هو التفرقة بمعنى التمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى نفس حركة الاختيار مع قطع النظر عن الأمور. وحاصله أن الوجوب والامتناع بحسب أخذ الفعل مع وصف الوجود أو العدم أو بحسب أن الله خلقه أو لم يخلقه لا ينافي تساوي الطرفين بالنظر إلى نفس القدرة.
The Fourth Objective: Proof of the created power and its definition. There is disagreement on the way to prove it. The truth is what the Ash'aris hold: that it is known and its existence is known by inner experience (wijdan), as we indicated: for the difference between one ascending voluntarily and one falling from a height is necessary, for we find the state of ascent is a fixed attribute for the ascender without the state of falling; likewise we find a necessary distinction between the movement of tremor and the movement of choice. As for the objection of the Imam that choice before the act is invalid in your view, and with it it is prevented because of the impossibility of non-existence at the moment of existence, and the occurrence of movement at the moment Allah creates it is necessary and before it impossible, so where is choice? Also, the occurrence of the act when motives are equal is impossible, and when they are not equal the preponderant is necessary and the preponderated is impossible, so capability is not established. The answer is to rebut by saying that these statements contradict intuition and everything that contradicts intuition is false, even if the detailed reason for its falsehood is not known. The solution is that we do not accept the impossibility of non-existence at the moment of existence, because it is possible for non-existence to occur instead of it, but conditional upon existence. The same holds for movement and the occurrence of the act when motives are equal, for the necessary is conditional upon its creation, not at the time of its creation. The impossible is conditional when motives are equal, not at the time of their equality. And the distinction is necessary with respect to the two movements themselves, for the movement of striking, with respect to its essence disregarding external matters, is voluntary unlike the movement of tremor. And it was said: likewise you find a necessary distinction between the movement of tremor and the movement of choice. And it was answered that the necessary is the distinction in the sense of being able to do and refrain, with respect to the voluntary movement itself disregarding other matters. The gist is that necessity and impossibility according to the act taken with the attribute of existence or non-existence, or according to whether Allah created it or not, does not contradict the equality of both sides with respect to the power itself.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof of the existence of human power (al-qudra al-haditha) through inner experience (al-wijdan). The proof relies on the immediate, necessary distinction we find between voluntary and involuntary acts: for example, the difference between going up a ladder by choice and falling from a height, or between a trembling movement and a deliberate movement. This distinction indicates that the agent has power over the voluntary act but not over the involuntary one. An objection from Imam (al-Razi) is then raised: choice before the act is invalid according to Ash'aris (since they hold power is simultaneous with the act), and if choice is simultaneous with the act then non-existence cannot coexist with existence, so where is choice? Also, the act when motives are equal is impossible, and when motives are unequal one side becomes necessary and the other impossible, so capability is not established. The answer is twofold: (1) a rebuttal that such objections contradict intuitive knowledge, which is more basic; (2) a detailed solution that impossibility is conditional, not absolute: non-existence can occur instead of existence under a condition, and necessity with respect to the act is only conditional upon divine creation, not in the time of creation. The passage clarifies that the distinction is between the movements themselves considered in their essence, and that necessity and impossibility do not negate the equality of possibilities with respect to power itself.

الوجدان
الفرق الضروري
حركة الاختيار
حركة الارتعاش
اعتراض الإمام
استواء الدواعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari proof of power is circular because it uses the concept of choice to prove power.
Correction: The Ash'aris appeal to immediate intuitive knowledge (wijdan) that distinguishes voluntary from involuntary acts, not a preconceived notion of choice.

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence for the existence of human power according to the Ash'aris?
A) Scriptural proof
B) Philosophical syllogism
C) Immediate inner experience (wijdan)
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Human power is known through immediate inner experience, not through abstract reasoning alone, and the Ash'ari response to objections preserves this intuitive knowledge.

Do you normally take that inner experience for granted, or does it remind you that your ability to act is a created gift from Allah? What would change if you paused to sense that gift before every action today?
?
The lesson distinguishes between voluntary and involuntary actions through immediate experience. Think of an area in your life where you feel trapped, as if your actions are involuntary—like a habit or fear. According to this passage, the distinction is necessary: you know you have power over voluntary acts. How does that truth challenge you to take responsibility for that area, rather than claiming helplessness?
?
Imam al-Razi's objection suggests that if power is only simultaneous with the act, genuine choice vanishes. But the Ash'ari responds that the distinction is based on the essence of the act itself, not on external conditions. Does your daily experience confirm that you truly have a say in your actions, even if you cannot always explain how? What one act today will you consciously perform to affirm that your power is real and accountable to Allah?

The Core Proof that Power Must Be Concurrent with the Act
Sanusi presents the core proof that power cannot precede its act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction that arises if a created power precedes its act, proving that power must be concurrent with the act.

Core Idea
Power cannot exist before its act, for then the act would be impossible.

Sanusi begins by establishing a foundational argument: if a created power existed before its act, a contradiction would ensue. This forces the conclusion that power and act are necessarily simultaneous. The following passage presents the core proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the core proof. Notice the conditional structure: 'if it were possible, let us suppose...'
The passage is short but dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the contradiction
The state we supposed to be prior to the act turns out to be the state of the act itself. This is the 'khalaf' (contradiction).
3
Understand the implication
Since the supposition leads to impossibility, the original premise (power preceding act) must be false. This establishes concurrency.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمتنع وجوده ،قبله، بل أمكن فلنفرض وجوده فيه (فهي) أي فالحالة التي فرضناها أنها حالة سابقة على الفعل ليست كذلك، بل هي حال الفعل، هذا خلف) محال والأوهام، فبقولنا في وقت يندفع النقض بالقدرة القديمة، فإنها قبل الفعل في الأزل أي في جميع الأزمنة الماضية الغير المتناهية، فلا يلزم من إمكان المقدور قبل حدوثه في وقت فرض وقوع المقدور فيه من الأوقات المتقدمة على وجوده ولا يمكن وجوده في جميع الأوقات حتى يلزم مقارنته للقدرة القديمة لامتناع قدمه فهو ليس مقدوراً باعتبار وجوده في جميع الأوقات الغير المتناهية وبقولنا : إذ لا قدرة بدون المقدور اندفع ما قيل أنه يجوز أن تكون القدرة في الحال متعلقة بوجود المقدور في ثاني الحال فإن وجود القدرة من غير أن يكون لها مقدور محال وإن جاز تقدمها على وجودها وبقولنا : المقدور ممتنع في ذلك الوقت اندفع الايراد بأنه يلزم أن يكون الامكان لازماً لماهية الممكن لأن امتناع الوجود في وقت مخصوص لا ينافي إمكان الوجود المطلق، وبقولنا : أي يمكن أن تكون القدرة المتقدمة على الفعل مقارنة له حال تقدمها غير متقدمة عليه حال فرض وقوعه في الزمان المتقدم على الفعل ولا استحالة فيه إذ التقدم حال عدم الفعل فيه لا ينافي عدم التقدم على تقدير فرض حصول الفعل فيه .
Its existence before it is impossible; but if it were possible, let us suppose it exists in it (so it) i.e., the state we supposed to be prior to the act is not so, but it is the state of the act, this is a contradiction) is impossible. And illusions, so by our saying 'at a time' the objection is repelled regarding the eternal power, for it is before the act in pre-eternity, i.e., in all infinite past times, so it does not follow from the possibility of the object of power before its occurrence at a time that the object of power occurs in some times preceding its existence, and it cannot exist in all times such that it would be simultaneous with the eternal power because of the impossibility of its eternity; so it is not an object of power in terms of its existence in all infinite times. And by our saying: there is no power without an object of power, what was said is repelled—that it is permissible for power in the present to be related to the existence of the object of power in the next moment, for the existence of power without having an object of power is impossible, even if its priority over its existence is permissible. And by our saying: the object of power is impossible at that time, the objection is repelled that possibility would be necessary for the essence of the possible, because impossibility of existence at a particular time does not negate absolute possibility of existence. And by our saying: it is possible that the power preceding the act is concurrent with it in the state of its priority, not preceding it in the state of supposing its occurrence in the time preceding the act, and there is no impossibility in it, for priority in the state of the absence of the act does not negate non-priority on the supposition of the act occurring in it.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument that a created power (qudra) cannot exist before its act, because if it did, the act would be impossible at that prior time, leading to a contradiction. The author refutes three potential objections. First, regarding eternal divine power: divine power exists from eternity and is related to temporal acts; this does not require the act to be eternal, so it does not entail the impossibility of the act before its time. Second, regarding the possibility of power without an object: power cannot exist without an object at all, so if no act is possible at that time, power cannot exist then. Third, regarding the objection that impossibility at a specific time does not negate absolute possibility: the author clarifies that the impossibility here is absolute because the act's existence at that prior time would be contradictory (since it would require being both prior and simultaneous). The argument proceeds by supposing power before the act and showing that this leads to a logical contradiction, thus proving that power must be concurrent with the act.

Key Terms
قدرة
مقدور
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can exist before the act as long as the act eventually happens.
Correction: The text shows that if power existed earlier, the act would be impossible at that earlier time, leading to a contradiction. Power must have an object at the moment it exists.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the supposition that power exists before the act?
A) The act becomes eternal
B) The act becomes impossible at that prior time
C) The act becomes necessary
D) The act becomes contingent

Key Takeaway
The core proof shows that supposing power precedes its act leads to an unavoidable contradiction, establishing that power must be concurrent with the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power is always concurrent with His act, does that mean every moment of your life is directly willed and powered by Him? How does this change the way you view your daily struggles and decisions?
?
You often postpone repentance or good deeds, thinking 'I still have time.' But if God's power does not precede the act, then His power is only in the present moment. What does that imply about the urgency of acting now?
?
When you sin, do you think God's power is 'absent' or 'delayed'? How does the truth that His power is always exactly concurrent with every act—including your sin—affect your sense of accountability before Him?

Refuting Objections to the Concurrency of Power and Act
Sanusi refutes three potential objections, using the phrase 'وبقولنا'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes three key objections to the concurrency of power and act, including the case of eternal power, power without object, and temporal impossibility.

Core Idea
Every objection is answered by clarifying that power cannot exist without its object being possible at that time.

After establishing the core proof, Sanusi anticipates and refutes several objections. These objections arise from considering divine eternal power, the possibility of power without an immediate object, and the distinction between absolute and temporal possibility. Each refutation reinforces the necessity of concurrency.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation section
Read from 'فبقولنا' to the end. Notice the repeated 'وبقولنا' introducing each refutation.
There are three main refutations, each introduced by 'وبقولنا'.
2
First refutation: eternal power
The objection claims divine power precedes acts eternally. Sanusi replies by noting that eternal power is not comparable to created power; the act is not eternal, so concurrency is not required.
3
Second refutation: power without object
The objection suggests power can exist without an object at the moment. Sanusi replies that power necessarily needs an object; thus if the act is impossible at that time, power cannot exist then.
4
Third refutation: temporal vs absolute possibility
The objection says impossibility at a specific time doesn't negate absolute possibility. Sanusi clarifies that the impossibility here is absolute due to the contradiction, so the act is not possible at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And illusions, so by our saying 'at a time' the objection is repelled regarding the eternal power, for it is before the act in pre-eternity, i.e., in all infinite past times, so it does not follow from the possibility of the object of power before its occurrence at a time that the object of power occurs in some times preceding its existence, and it cannot exist in all times such that it would be simultaneous with the eternal power because of the impossibility of its eternity; so it is not an object of power in terms of its existence in all infinite times. ?

?

Absolute Impossibility and the Meaning of Contradiction
Commentator clarifies the meaning of absolute impossibility and defines contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator distinguishes absolute impossibility from temporal impossibility and defines 'contradiction' (خلف) in the context of the proof.

Core Idea
The proof's impossibility is absolute, not conditional, and contradiction is a logical violation.

The commentator begins by clarifying key phrases from the earlier matn. He emphasizes that the phrase 'its existence in it is impossible' refers to absolute impossibility, not just temporal impossibility. He then defines what 'contradiction' (خلف) means in this logical context, showing that it is not a simple assumption but a logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the commentator's clarifications.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the terms 'امتناع الوجود المطلق' and 'خلف' and how they are used.
3
Understand the nuance
Recognize that the commentator is correcting a potential misunderstanding by distinguishing absolute impossibility from conditional or temporal impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل يمتنع وجوده فيه التقييد بالجار والمجرور إشارة إلى ما قلنا من أنه امتناع الوجود المطلق وقوله : محال تفسير الخلف بخلاف المفروض، لأن كون الحالة السابقة حال الفعل ليس خلاف المفروض إذ اللازم اتحادها به على تقدير فرض حصول الفعل فيه والمفروض سبقيتها على الفعل على تقدير عدم حصول الفعل فيه . قوله : (أي فالحالة إلخ) لم يرجع الضمير إلى القدرة بأن يكون قوله حال الفعل منصوب على الظرفية لأن كون القدرة في حال الفعل متفرع على كون الحالة السابقة حال الفعل، فلا بد من اعتباره . كما ذهب إلى مثله من قال : بتقدم قدرة الله تعالى ذاتاً، وحدوث تعلقها الموجب للمراد وإن بني الكلام على اعتراف الخصم بتقدم التعلق أيضاً كان الدليل الزامياً لا برهاناً، ويمكن أن يجاب بأن الكلام مبني على عدم بقاء قدرة العبد عندنا فلا يجوز تأخر تعلقها عنها، وأما رابعاً : فلجواز تعلقها في الحال بوجود المقدور في الاستقبال، ولا حاجة في هذا إلى توسيط الايقاع ولا إلى إمكان الفعل في الحال، اللهم إلا أن يقال : تعلق القدرة بالمستحيل حين التعلق ممتنع، سواء كان ممكناً مال أم لا . قوله : (فهي أي فالحلة إلخ يمكن أن يرجع ضمير هي إلى القدرة بأن يكون قوله حال ) الفعل نصباً على الظرفية فيكون حاصله ما ذكره في شرح المقاصد، وهو أنه لو كانت القدرة قبل الفعل لكان الفعل قبل وقوعه ممكناً ، لكنه محال لكن ما ذكره الشارح أنسب مع أنها المذكورة صريحاً فيما سبق، لأن كون القدرة مع الفعل على فرض قبليتها، وإن كان خلفاً محالاً، إلا أنه لا معنى لجعله دليلا على امتناع الفعل قبل نفسه سيما عند ظهور الدليل الذي قرره الشارح كما لا يخفى . لأن كون المتقدم على الفعل مقارناً له يستلزم اجتماع النقيضين، أعني كونه متقدماً وغير متقدم فقد لزم من وجود الفعل قبله محال فلا يكون ممكناً إذ الممكن لا يستلزم المستحيل بالذات، وإذا لم يكن الفعل ممكناً قبله لم يكن مقدورا قبله، فلا تكون القدرة عليه موجودة حينئذ ، ولا شك أن وجود القدرة بعد الفعل، مما لا فتعين أن تكون موجودة معه وهو المطلوب . قوله : ( وإذا لم يكن الفعل ممكناً (قبله أي بالنسبة إلى القدرة الحادثة لم يكن مقدوراً لها قوله : ( فلا تكون القدرة عليه أي على الفعل موجودة إذ وجود القدرة في زمان لا مقدور فيه أصلاً محال وإن كان وجودها بدون المقدور ممكناً، بل متحققاً كما في القدرة القديمة لأن القدرة صفة بها يتمكن الفعل والترك فلو لم يكن مما يمكن عليه لم تكن القدرة متحققة.
His saying: 'Rather, its existence in it is impossible'—the restriction with the prepositional phrase indicates what we said, that it is absolute impossibility of existence. And his saying: 'impossible' is an interpretation of contradiction contrary to the assumed, because the prior state being the state of the act is not contrary to the assumed, since the necessary is its unification with it on the assumption of the act occurring in it, while the assumed is its priority over the act on the assumption of the act not occurring in it. His saying: 'i.e., the state, etc.'—the pronoun does not refer to power such that his saying 'the state of the act' is accusative as an adverb of time, because power being in the state of the act is derived from the prior state being the state of the act, so it must be considered. Likewise, one who held that the power of God, exalted, is prior in essence, and that its relation which necessitates the willed is originated, and if the discourse is based on the opponent's admission of the priority of relation as well, then the proof is only ad hominem, not demonstrative. It can be answered that the discourse is based on the non-continuance of the servant's power in our view, so its relation cannot be delayed from it. And fourth, because it is permissible for it to be related in the present to the existence of the object of power in the future, and there is no need in this for mediating the causing-to-occur nor for the possibility of the act in the present, unless it is said: the relation of power to the impossible at the time of relation is impossible, whether it is possible or not. His saying: 'so it, i.e., the state, etc.'—the pronoun 'she' can refer to power, such that his saying 'state of the act' is accusative as an adverb, so its result is what was mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid, that if power were before the act, the act would be possible before its occurrence, but that is impossible. However, what the commentator mentioned is more appropriate, even though it is mentioned explicitly earlier, because power being with the act on the assumption of its priority, even if it is an impossible contradiction, there is no point in making it a proof of the impossibility of the act before itself, especially given the evidence established by the commentator, as is not hidden. Because the prior being concurrent with the act entails the combination of two contradictories, namely its being prior and non-prior; so from the existence of the act before it, an impossibility follows, so it is not possible, for the possible does not entail the impossible by itself. And if the act is not possible before it, it is not an object of power before it, so power does not exist over it at that time; and there is no doubt that the existence of power after the act is [not possible], so it must exist with it, and that is what is sought. His saying: 'And if the act is not possible' before it, i.e., relative to the created power, it is not an object of power for it. His saying: 'So power does not exist over it', i.e., over the act, because existence of power at a time with no object of power at all is impossible, even if existence without an object of power is possible—indeed realized—as in the eternal power, because power is an attribute by which one can do or refrain; so if it is not something over which one can act, power is not realized.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary by the author (sharh) on the previous argument. It clarifies the precise meaning of 'its existence in it is impossible' as absolute impossibility, not merely temporal. It discusses the pronoun reference in the earlier text, noting that the pronoun 'she' in 'so it' could refer to power, but the commentator prefers it to refer to the state. The passage also addresses an objection that the proof might be merely ad hominem if based on the opponent's concession, but the author replies that in our school (Ash'ari), the servant's power does not persist, so its relation cannot be delayed. A fourth point is added: power can be related to a future act without needing an intermediate 'iqa''. The commentator then explains the core logical reasoning: if something prior to the act is concurrent with it, that entails a contradiction of being both prior and non-prior, making the act impossible before it, thus power cannot exist before the act. The passage concludes by emphasizing that power without an object is impossible for created power, although divine power can exist without an object.

Key Terms
تعلق
خلف
امتناع الوجود المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'its existence in it is impossible' refers to a temporal impossibility or a conditional impossibility.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states it is absolute impossibility of existence (امتناع الوجود المطلق), not conditional on time or circumstances.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the phrase 'بل يمتنع وجوده فيه' indicate?
A) Temporal impossibility
B) Absolute impossibility of existence
C) Conditional impossibility
D) Logical possibility

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of the act's existence before its time is absolute, not conditional, and the concept of 'contradiction' (خلف) is defined as a logical violation, not a mere assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's power is absolute and not bound by any conditions, how would that change the way you pray for what seems impossible in your life today? Write down one specific situation you have treated as 'conditionally impossible' and commit to asking Allah for it tonight.
?
The commentator defines contradiction (خلف) as being contrary to the assumed. Where in your own life do you hold assumptions about Allah that are actually contradictory to what He has revealed? For example, assuming He will not forgive a repeated sin. Read the source again: the contradiction is logical violation. How will you align your assumptions with the truth this week?
?
This lesson distinguishes between absolute and conditional impossibility. Are you treating any of your own limitations as absolute (e.g., 'I can never overcome this sin') when they are only conditional? What would it mean to truly accept that with Allah nothing is absolutely impossible? What step will you take tomorrow to act on that belief?

Defending the Proof: Ad Hominem Objection and the Fourth Point
Commentator refutes ad hominem objection and introduces fourth point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the proof against the charge of being ad hominem and explains the fourth point about power relating to a future act.

Core Idea
The proof is demonstrative, not ad hominem, and power can relate to a future act without intermediate steps.

The commentator addresses a potential objection that the proof relies on the opponent's concession and is therefore merely ad hominem. He argues that on the Ash'ari view, the servant's power does not persist, so the relation cannot be delayed. He then adds a fourth point: power can be related to a future act without requiring an 'iqa'' or present possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Identify the ad hominem charge and how the commentator responds.
2
Analyze the fourth point
Understand the commentator's additional argument about power relating to a future act.
3
Discuss the conditions
Consider the exception about power relating to the impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اتحادها به على تقدير فرض حصول الفعل فيه والمفروض سبقيتها على الفعل على تقدير عدم حصول الفعل فيه . قوله : (أي فالحالة إلخ) لم يرجع الضمير إلى القدرة بأن يكون قوله حال الفعل منصوب على الظرفية لأن كون القدرة في حال الفعل متفرع على كون الحالة السابقة حال الفعل، فلا بد من اعتباره . كما ذهب إلى مثله من قال : بتقدم قدرة الله تعالى ذاتاً، وحدوث تعلقها الموجب للمراد وإن بني الكلام على اعتراف الخصم بتقدم The Logical Proof: Prior State Concurrent with Act Leads to Contradiction
Commentator proves by contradiction that power must be concurrent with the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reasoning that if power were prior to the act, it would entail a contradiction, and thus power must be concurrent with the act.

Core Idea
If power before the act is concurrent with it, that entails the contradiction of being both prior and non-prior.

The commentator now lays out the core logical argument. He explains that if we assume power is prior to the act, and then suppose the act occurs, that would make the prior state concurrent with the act, leading to a contradiction (being both prior and non-prior). Since the possible cannot entail an impossibility, the act cannot be possible before it, and thus power cannot exist before the act. This forces the conclusion that power must be concurrent with the act.

Guided Path
1
Trace the conditional chain
Follow the 'if... then...' chain: if power before act, then act possible before occurrence, but that leads to contradiction.
2
Identify the contradiction
Understand why prior being concurrent is a contradiction (being both prior and non-prior).
3
Conclude the necessity
Since the possible cannot entail an impossibility, the act is not possible before, so power cannot be before, so power must be with the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اتحادها به على تقدير فرض حصول الفعل فيه والمفروض سبقيتها على الفعل على تقدير عدم حصول الفعل فيه . قوله : (أي فالحالة إلخ) لم يرجع الضمير إلى القدرة بأن يكون قوله حال الفعل منصوب على الظرفية لأن كون And if the act is not possible before it, it is not an object of power before it, so power does not exist over it at that time; and there is no doubt that the existence of power after the act is [not possible], so it must exist with it, and that is what is sought. His saying: 'And if the act is not possible' before it, i.e., relative to the created power, it is not an object of power for it. His saying: 'So power does not exist over it', i.e., over the act, because existence of power at a time with no object of power at all is impossible, even if existence without an object of power is possible—indeed realized—as in the eternal power, because power is an attribute by which one can do or refrain; so if it is not something over which one can act, power is not realized.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary by the author (sharh) on the previous argument. It clarifies the precise meaning of 'its existence in it is impossible' as absolute impossibility, not merely temporal. It discusses the pronoun reference in the earlier text, noting that the pronoun 'she' in 'so it' could refer to power, but the commentator prefers it to refer to the state. The passage also addresses an objection that the proof might be merely ad hominem if based on the opponent's concession, but the author replies that in our school (Ash'ari), the servant's power does not persist, so its relation cannot be delayed. A fourth point is added: power can be related to a future act without needing an intermediate 'iqa''. The commentator then explains the core logical reasoning: if something prior to the act is concurrent with it, that entails a contradiction of being both prior and non-prior, making the act impossible before it, thus power cannot exist before the act. The passage concludes by emphasizing that power without an object is impossible for created power, although divine power can exist without an object.

Key Terms
نقيضين
قدرة قديمة
قدرة حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes what it wants to prove.
Correction: The commentator shows the proof is not circular; it is based on the logical impossibility of the act before itself being possible due to the contradiction that follows.

Checkpoint
What does the act being impossible before its occurrence entail for power?
A) Power must be before
B) Power must be after
C) Power must be concurrent
D) Power can be before or after

Key Takeaway
The logical impossibility of the act before itself forces the conclusion that power must exist concurrently with the act, and while divine power can exist without an object, created power cannot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that your ability to do something does not precede the action; it comes at the moment. Consider an obligation you have been putting off. Are you assuming your power to perform it will remain when you finally decide to act? What does this logical proof demand of you right now?
?
The proof distinguishes created power from Allah's power. In your daily life, do you act as if your power is self-sufficient or as if it is given to you moment by moment? What would change if you lived with constant awareness that your ability to do good is only present at the moment of action?

Objection and Answer: Power Related to Future Acts (Iqa')
Objection that power can be related to causing future acts, and the response distinguishing two types of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that power can relate to future acts via a 'causing-to-occur' (iqa') and the commentator's response distinguishing two types of relation.

Core Idea
Power has two relations: one essential for ability and one that produces existence.

This passage presents a subtle objection regarding how divine or human power can relate to acts that occur in the future. The objector argues that power can exist prior to the act without contradiction if it is directed toward the act's occurrence in a later moment. The commentator clarifies that this objection confuses two distinct types of relation, and that the discussion is about the conceptual relation, which cannot be delayed. This distinction is crucial for understanding causation and power in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the objection claiming that power can exist prior to the act by being related to the act's occurrence in the second moment. Note the key term 'iqa'' (causing-to-occur).
2
Identify the two types of relation
The passage introduces a conceptual relation (talluq ma'nawi) that defines ability and cannot be delayed, and an existential relation (talluq wujudi) that produces the act and can be delayed. Understand their definitions.
The conceptual relation is what distinguishes an agent as capable of doing or leaving the act.
3
Trace the commentator's refutation
See why the objection fails: it conflates the two relations. The discussion is about the conceptual relation, which must exist as long as the agent has power. The objector's scenario only works for the existential relation.
4
Note the example of eternal divine power
The commentator uses God's eternal power as an example: it is always present but does not require its objects to exist in pre-eternity. This illustrates the existential relation being delayed.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Power in the present can be related to a future causing-to-occur, but this does not entail the act's possibility in the present. The conceptual relation remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن قيل إلخ) منع لقوله فلا تكون القدرة عليه موجودة فيه أنه يجوز أن تكون القدرة في الزمان السابق على وجود المقدور موجودة مع عدم امكان الفعل في ذلك الحال بناء، على أن يكون تعلقها في ثاني الحال وامكان المقدور ، إنما يستلزم التعلق لأنها حال وجود الفعل لا حال وجود القدرة ، فلا يلزم ما ذكر من المحال من كون القدرة متقدمة وغير متقدمة هذا على طبق ما قرره الشارح، ويرد عليه أن التعرض للايقاع حينئذ مستدرك إذ يكفي أن يقال : إن القدرة على حصول الفعل في ثاني الحال وهو لا يستدعي امكانه في الحال إلى آخر. وإن للقدرة تعلقين تعلق معنوي يقال : فلان قادر عليه أي متمكن من فعله وتركه وبه يميز المقدور بالنسبة إلى القادر وهذا لا يمكن تأخيره عن القدرة وهو المراد من قولنا فيما سبق انتفاء المقدور يستلزم انتفاء القدرة، وسيجيء في كلام الشارح من أن وجود القدرة بدون هذا التعلق مما يأباه البديهة وتعلق يترتب عليه الوجود وهو متأخر عن تعلق الإرادة، ويجوز تأخره عن وجود القدرة والكلام في الأول دون الثاني، وتقرير هذا الاعتراض عندي إن القدرة السابقة، إنما تستدعي امکان حصول الفعل في الزمان السابق لو كانت متقدمة على الفعل في ثاني الحال، أما إذا كانت على ايقاع الفعل في ثاني الحال، فلا يستدعي امكان الايقاع الذي في ثاني الحال، ولا يستدعي . الفعل في الحال، ولا يلزم من امكان الايقاع المذكور في الحال امكان الفعل في الحال حتى يلزم المحال، ألا ترى أن القدرة القديمة على ايقاع المقدورات مما لا تزال محققة في الأزل مع امتناع وجود المقدورات فيه وعلى هذا الجواب يرد بأن الايقاع في ثاني الحال أما نفس حصول الفعل في الوجود، فهو محال في الحال كالحصول فلا يكون متعلق القدرة بالتعلق المعنوي، وأما غيره فيحتاج إلى إيقاع آخر لأنه ممكن حال حاصل بتأثير القدرة في ظاهره مطابقة للسؤال بلا ريبة، وأما ما قيل في تقريره من أن القدرة متعلقة بالايقاع المتقدم على الوقوع زماناً فيكفي إمكان الايقاع . قوله : ( فإن قيل إلخ) حاصله أن القدرة في الحال متعلقة بالايقاع المتقدم على الوقوع زماناً فيكفي إمكان الايقاع في الحال ولا يستدعي هذا امكان الوقوع فيها، فتأمل.
His saying: 'For if it is said, etc.' is a denial of his statement that so power does not exist over it at that time—that it is permissible for power in the time prior to the existence of the object of power to exist with the impossibility of the act in that state, based on its relation being in the second state and the possibility of the object of power; it only necessitates the relation because it is the state of the existence of the act, not the state of the existence of power; so the mentioned impossibility of power being prior and non-prior does not follow. This is in accordance with what the commentator established. And it is replied to that the reference to causing-to-occur (iqa') then is superfluous, for it suffices to say: power over the occurrence of the act in the second state does not require its possibility in the present, etc. And indeed power has two relations: a conceptual relation (talluq ma'nawi) by which it is said 'so-and-so is able to do it', i.e., capable of doing it and leaving it, and by it the object of power is distinguished relative to the agent, and this cannot be delayed from power, and that is what is meant by our earlier statement that the absence of the object of power entails the absence of power. And there will come in the commentator's speech that the existence of power without this relation is something that the intellect rejects. And a relation that produces existence (talluq yutarattab 'alayhi al-wujud), which is posterior to the relation of will, and it is permissible for it to be delayed from the existence of power; and the discussion is about the first, not the second. And my formulation of this objection is that prior power only requires the possibility of the act occurring in the prior time if it were prior to the act in the second state; but if it is for causing the act to occur in the second state, it does not require the possibility of the causing-to-occur in the second state, nor does it require the act in the present, and it does not follow from the possibility of the mentioned causing-to-occur in the present the possibility of the act in the present such that the impossible would follow. Do you not see that the eternal power over causing the objects of power to occur is always realized in pre-eternity despite the impossibility of the objects of power existing in it? And on this answer it is replied that the causing-to-occur in the second state: either it is the very occurrence of the act in existence, and that is impossible in the present like the occurrence itself, so it cannot be the object of power by the conceptual relation; or it is something else, and then it needs another act of causing-to-occur because it is a possible state produced by the effect of power in its outward appearance, matching the question without doubt. And as for what was said in its formulation, that power is related to the causing-to-occur that precedes the occurrence in time, so the possibility of that causing-to-occur suffices. His saying: 'For if it is said, etc.' — its gist is that power in the present is related to the causing-to-occur that precedes the occurrence in time, so the possibility of that causing-to-occur in the present suffices, and this does not require the possibility of occurrence in it. So reflect.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a detailed response regarding whether power can be related to an act in the future via an intermediate 'causing-to-occur' (iqa'). The objector argues that power can exist prior to the act if it is related to the act's occurrence in the second moment, not requiring the act's possibility at the time of power. The commentator responds that this objection is based on a conflation of two distinct types of relation (talluq): a conceptual relation that is essential for ability (tamakkun) and cannot be delayed, and a relation that produces existence, which can be delayed. The discussion is about the first type. The commentator also notes that even eternal divine power, which is always present, does not require the existence of its objects in pre-eternity. The objection is further refuted by analyzing the nature of 'causing-to-occur': if it is identical to the act's occurrence, it is impossible in the present; if it is something else, it requires another act of causing. The passage ends by summarizing that power in the present can be related to a future causing-to-occur, but this does not entail the act's possibility in the present.

تعلق وجودي
إيقاع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power can be related to a future act without any conceptual relation, simply by being directed at the future occurrence.
Correction: The author distinguishes between a conceptual relation (essential for ability, cannot be delayed) and an existential relation (can be delayed). The objection only works for the existential relation, not the conceptual one.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what two types of relation does power have?
A) Temporal and eternal
B) Conceptual and existential
C) Necessary and contingent
D) Divine and human

Key Takeaway
Power's conceptual relation to an act is essential for ability and cannot be delayed; any objection conflating it with the existential relation is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I can do it later' or 'I have the ability to change, just not right now.' But this lesson teaches that true ability (the conceptual relation of power) cannot be delayed from your present state. In what area of your life — a relationship, a habit, a duty — are you deceiving yourself by claiming you are 'able' in the future when you lack the real capacity now? What would it mean to honestly admit your powerlessness today?
?
The objector tried to find a loophole by focusing on 'causing' rather than the act itself. Where in your spiritual life do you substitute a process or intention for the actual deed — convincing yourself that planning to pray is enough, or that meaning to apologize is the same as truly being able to do so? What act have you been avoiding by hiding behind a conceptual smokescreen?
?
The passage says the conceptual relation 'cannot be delayed from power.' Your power to obey Allah, to do good, or to repent is defined by your present reality, not by potential. If your conceptual relation to a good act is absent now, then you are not truly able — you are merely hoping. What specific act of obedience have you convinced yourself you are 'able' to do but have never actualized? What step can you take today to move from conceptual possibility to existential commitment, knowing that the first relation must be real now?
